HEADLINE: THREATS AND RESPONSES; 
In the President's Words: 'Free People Will Keep the Peace of the World'

 BODY:
Following is a transcript of the speech given yesterday by President Bush to the American Enterprise Institute in Washington, as recorded by The New York Times:
 We meet here during a crucial period in the history of our nation and of the civilized world. Part of that history was written by others; the rest will be written by us. 
 On a September morning, threats that had gathered for years in secret and far away led to murder in our country on a massive scale. As a result, we must look at security in a new way, because our country is a battlefield in the first war of the 21st century. 
We learned a lesson: the dangers of our time must be confronted actively and forcefully before we see them again in our skies and in our cities. And we set a goal: we will not allow the triumph of hatred and violence in the affairs of men.
Our coalition of more than 90 countries is pursuing the networks of terror with every tool of law enforcement and with military power. We have arrested or otherwise dealt with many key commanders of Al Qaeda. Across the world, we are hunting down the killers one by one. We are winning, and we're showing them the definition of American justice. And we are opposing the greatest danger in the war on terror -- outlaw regimes arming with weapons of mass destruction. 
In Iraq, a dictator is building and hiding weapons that could enable him to dominate the Middle East and intimidate the civilized world, and we will not allow it. This same tyrant has close ties to terrorist organizations and could supply them with the terrible means to strike this country, and America will not permit it.
The danger posed by Saddam Hussein and his weapons cannot be ignored or wished away. The danger must be confronted. We hope that the Iraqi regime will meet the demands of the United Nations and disarm fully and peacefully. If it does not, we are prepared to disarm Iraq by force. Either way, this danger will be removed.
The safety of the American people depends on ending this direct and growing threat. Acting against the danger will also contribute greatly to the long-term safety and stability of our world. The current Iraqi regime has shown the power of tyranny to spread discord and violence in the Middle East. A liberated Iraq can show the power of freedom to transform that vital region by bringing hope and progress into the lives of millions. America's interest in security and America's belief in liberty both lead in the same direction -- to a free and peaceful Iraq.
The first to benefit from a free Iraq would be the Iraqi people themselves. Today they live in scarcity and fear under a dictator who has brought them nothing but war and misery and torture. Their lives and their freedom matter little to Saddam Hussein. But Iraqi lives and freedom matter greatly to us. 
Bringing stability and unity to a free Iraq will not be easy. Yet that is no excuse to leave the Iraqi regime's torture chambers and poison labs in operation. Any future the Iraqi people choose for themselves will be better than the nightmare world that Saddam Hussein has chosen for them. 
If we must use force, the United States and our coalition stand ready to help the citizens of a liberated Iraq. We will deliver medicine to the sick. And we are now moving into place nearly three million emergency rations to feed the hungry. We will make sure that Iraq's 55,000 food distribution sites, operating under the oil-for-food program, are stocked and open as soon as possible. The United States and Great Britain are providing tens of millions of dollars to the U.N. High Commission on Refugees and to such groups as the World Food Program and Unicef to provide emergency aid to the Iraqi people.
We will alsolead in carrying out the urgent and dangerous work of destroying chemical and biological weapons. We will provide security against those who try to spread chaos or settle scores or threaten the territorial integrity of Iraq. We will seek to protect Iraq's natural resources from sabotage by a dying regime, and ensure those resources are used for the benefit of the owners: the Iraqi people.
The United States has no intention of determining the precise form of Iraq's new government. That choice belongs to the Iraqi people. Yet we will ensure that one brutal dictator is not replaced by another. All Iraqis must have a voice in the new government, and all citizens must have their rights protected.
Rebuilding Iraq will require a sustained commitment from many nations, including our own. We will remain in Iraq as long as necessary and not a day more. America has made and kept this kind of commitment before in the peace that followed a world war. After defeating enemies, we did not leave behind occupying armies, we left constitutions and parliaments. We established an atmosphere of safety in which responsible, reform-minded local leaders could build lasting institutions of freedom. In societies that once bred fascism and militarism, liberty found a permanent home.
There was a time when many said that the cultures of Japan and Germany were incapable of sustaining democratic values. Well, they were wrong. Some say the same of Iraq today. They are mistaken. The nation of Iraq, with its proud heritage, abundant resources and skilled and educated people is fully capable of moving toward democracy and living in freedom. 
The world has a clear interest in the spread of democratic values, because stable and free nations do not breed the ideologies of murder. They encourage the peaceful pursuit of a better life. And there are hopeful signs of the desire for freedom in the Middle East. Arab intellectuals have called on Arab governments to address the freedom gap so their peoples can fully share in the progress of our times. Leaders in the region speak of a new Arab charter that champions internal reform, greater political participation, economic openness and free trade. And from Morocco to Bahrain and beyond, nations are taking genuine steps toward political reform. A new regime in Iraq would serve as a dramatic and inspiring example of freedom for other nations in the region.
It is presumptuous and insulting to suggest that a whole region of the world or the one-fifth of humanity that is Muslim is somehow untouched by the most basic aspirations of life. Human cultures can be vastly different, yet the human heart desires the same good things everywhere on earth. In our desire to be safe from brutal and bullying oppression, human beings are the same. In our desire to care for our children and give them a better life, we're the same. For these fundamental reasons, freedom and democracy will always and everywhere have greater appeal than the slogans of hatred and the tactics of terror.
Success in Iraq could also begin a new stage for Middle Eastern peace and set in motion progress towards a truly democratic Palestinian state. The passing of Saddam Hussein's regime will deprive terrorist networks of a wealthy patron that pays for terrorist training and offers rewards to families of suicide bombers.
And other regimes will be given a clear warning that support for terror will not be tolerated. Without this outside support for terrorism, Palestinians who are working for reform and long for democracy will be in a better position to choose new leaders --true leaders who strive for peace, true leaders who faithfully serve the people. A Palestinian state must be a reformed and peaceful state that abandons forever the use of terror.
For its part the new government of Israel, as the terror threat is removed and security improves, will be expected to support the creation of a viable Palestinian state and to work as quickly as possible toward a final status agreement. As progress is made toward peace, settlement activity in the occupied territories must end.
And the Arab states will be expected to meet their responsibilities to oppose terrorism, to support the emergence of a peaceful and democratic Palestine, and state clearly they will live in peace with Israel.
The United States and other nations are working on a road map for peace. We are setting out the necessary conditions for progress toward the goal of two states, Israel and Palestine, living side by side in peace and security. It is the commitment of our government and my personal commitment to implement the road map and to reach that goal. Old patterns of conflict in the Middle East can be broken if all concerned will let go of bitterness and hatred and violence and get on with the serious work of economic development and political reform and reconciliation. America will seize every opportunity in pursuit of peace, and the end of the present regime in Iraq would create such an opportunity. 
In confronting Iraq, the United States is also showing our commitment to effective international institutions. We're a permanent member of the United Nations Security Council. We helped to create the Security Council. 
We believe in the Security Council so much that we want its words to have meaning. The global threat of proliferation of weapons of mass destruction cannot be confronted by one nation alone. The world needs today, and will need tomorrow, international bodies with the authority and the will to stop the spread of terror and chemical and biological and nuclear weapons. A threat to all must be answered by all. High-minded pronouncements against proliferation mean little unless the strongest nations are willing to stand behind them and use force if necessary. After all, the United Nations was created, as Winston Churchill said, to make sure that the force of right will in the ultimate issue be protected by the right of force.
Another resolution is now before the Security Council. If the Council responds to Iraq's defiance with more excuses and delays, if all its authority proves to be empty, the United Nations will be severely weakened as a source of stability and order. If the members rise to this moment, then the Council will fulfill its founding purpose.
I've listened carefully as people and leaders around the world have made known their desire for peace. All of us want peace. The threat to peace does not come from those who seek to enforce the just demands of the civilized world. The threat to peace comes from those who flout those demands. If we have to act, we will act to restrain the violent and defend the cause of peace. And by acting we will signal to outlaw regimes that in this new century the boundaries of civilized behavior will be respected. 
Protecting those boundaries carries a cost. If war is forced upon us by Iraq's refusal to disarm, we will meet an enemy who hides his military forces behind civilians, who has terrible weapons, who is capable of any crime. These dangers are real, as our soldiers and sailors, airmen and marines fully understand. Yet no military has ever been better prepared to meet these challenges. 
Members of our armed forces also understand why they may be called to fight. They know that retreat before a dictator guarantees even greater sacrifices in the future. They know that America's cause is right and just: the liberty for an oppressed people and security for the American people. 
And I know something about these men and women who wear our uniform. They will complete every mission they are given with skill and honor and courage.
Much is asked of America in this year 2003. The work ahead is demanding. It will be difficult to help freedom take hold in a country that has known three decades of dictatorship, secret police, internal divisions and war. It'll be difficult to cultivate liberty and peace in the Middle East after so many generations of strife, yet the security of our nation and the hope of millions depend on us. And Americans do not turn away from duties because they are hard. We have met great tests in other times and we will meet the tests of our time.
We go forward with confidence because we trust in the power of human freedom to change lives and nations. By the resolve and purpose of America and of our friends and allies, we will make this an age of progress and liberty. Free people will set the course of history and free people will keep the peace of the world.
Thank you all very much.

HEADLINE: Senator Named to Delaware River Authority 

BYLINE:  By LAURA MANSNERUS 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Feb. 26 

 BODY:
Gov. James E. McGreevey today named a Republican state senator, John J. Matheussen, to lead the Delaware River Port Authority, removing a popular Republican from the evenly divided Senate. 
 Senator Matheussen's district in South Jersey has become an increasingly Democratic area, and could be closely contested in the legislative elections this year as both parties focus on taking control of the Senate. The State Assembly is comfortably controlled by the Democrats. 
 Nonetheless, Republicans expect to replace Mr. Matheussen with another well-known legislator, George F. Geist, a six-term Assembly member who was the top vote-getter among the four candidates who ran for the district's two Assembly seats in 2001.
If Mr. McGreevey's recommendation is accepted by the Port Authority board, Mr. Matheussen will become the chief executive of the bistate authority. By tradition, the governor of New Jersey appoints the chief executive and the governor of Pennsylvania chooses the chairman of the 16-member board. Last week, Pennsylvania's new governor, Edward G. Rendell, named himself chairman.
The authority controls bridges, commuter lines and ferries linking New Jersey and Pennsylvania, and it oversees port and economic development projects that involve millions of dollars in contracts. 
Mr. Matheussen, 50, a lawyer, was elected to the Senate in 1992. Last spring, he ran unsuccessfully for the Republican nomination for the United States Senate.
Mr. Geist's margin of victory in the legislative district, which includes parts of Camden and Gloucester Counties, was smaller than Mr. Matheussen's in the last election, but the two have run successfully as a team from their first legislative races.
If Mr. Matheussen is approved by the authority and resigns from the Senate, which now has 20 members of each party, his replacement will be chosen by the district's Republican county committees and will then run in the June 3 primary. All members of the Legislature are up for re-election this year. 
Gail Peterson, the Republican chairwoman in Camden County, said today, "I think everybody will come together to unite behind Assemblyman Geist."
Mr. Geist, 47, also a lawyer, said that he was "honored and excited" that party officials were encouraging him to seek the Senate seat but that he could not comment further.

HEADLINE: Labor Secretary's Talk Angers Union Leaders 

BYLINE:  By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: HOLLYWOOD, Fla., Feb 26 

 BODY:
Labor Secretary Elaine L. Chao ignited a furor today among labor leaders, including the administration's strongest friend in labor, the teamster president James P. Hoffa, when she cited repeated examples of union corruption while addressing the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s winter meeting. 
 Mr. Hoffa criticized Secretary Chao and said labor should back a presidential candidate who understands working people, causing many union leaders to say Mr. Hoffa was distancing himself from the president. 
Mr. Hoffa complained that the Bush administration was veering further to the right, and one of his top aides said the secretary's remarks were only the latest of several antilabor actions committed by the administration. 
Secretary Chao stunned more than 100 labor leaders when, in a response to a question about greater financial disclosure, she read aloud for several minutes about seven criminal cases involving officials from the machinists' union. She brought to the meeting a dossier detailing union-related crimes and she read from it after Thomas Buffenbarger, the machinists' president, asked her why the Bush administration was proposing far stricter financial disclosure rules for labor unions.
"It seemed like a clear attempt at intimidation," said Bruce Raynor, president of the Union of Needletrades, Industrial and Textile Employees. "A lot of people were already feeling angry at the Bush administration, and what she said made things a lot worse."
After Ms. Chao left the meeting, Mr. Hoffa -- whom Mr. Bush once invited into the first lady's box during a State of the Union address -- joined several others in voicing bitterness with the administration.
The meeting was closed to the news media, and Teamster officials said Mr. Hoffa, who was on a plane, was unavailable to expand on his comments. But Mike Mathis, the Teamsters' political director, said Mr. Hoffa was highly critical of the administration today and had grown increasingly exasperated with moves he views as antiworker, including proposing to open the border to Mexican trucks and tax cuts that give more to the rich.
Mr. Mathis said Mr. Hoffa told the labor leaders: "We worked very hard to develop a relationship with this administration and we have. But especially since the election last November we have seen this administration veer to the right and try to move issues that are antilabor."
Mr. Hoffa added, Mr. Mathis said: "We're disappointed frankly that they don't understand labor issues and labor people. The way the secretary presented herself today was proof of that. We think it's important that the labor movement strengthen its resolve as we go into 2004 to elect a president who understands working people."
Mr. Mathis said Mr. Hoffa, whose union represents 1.4 million workers, had not ruled out backing Mr. Bush. But he added: "The fact is Jim is disappointed with the direction of the administration. Bush has veered to the right since the election, and if he veers back on track, that will change things. But if Bush is not going to do that, we're going to try very hard to beat him. If there's a better Democratic candidate then that's who the Teamsters will support." 
Speaking about Mr. Hoffa, Morton Bahr, president of the Communications Workers of America, said: "He said that Secretary Chao's remarks just give us more reason to turn this administration out in 2004. That reflects everybody's attitude here."
At her own news conference after her speech, Ms. Chao defended the administration's labor record, but acknowledged there were some strains with union leaders.
"It's never easy," she said. "I pride myself on keeping an open door and good communications channels."
She added, "We want to work with those who want to work with us."
Ms. Chao said that Mr. Bush's policies were good for American workers, saying that his economic plan would create 1.4 million jobs. 
Several labor leaders said they were irritated by what they described as the secretary's confrontational tone. They said they were also upset that she said the economy was too weak to raise the federal minimum wage and declined to say much about an administration study that might reduce the number of workers who qualify for overtime pay. 
John J. Sweeney, president of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., which represents 13 million union members, said Ms. Chao's remarks were insulting.
"I have never seen a secretary of labor who is so antilabor," Mr. Sweeney said at a news conference. "It's fair to say what we saw from the secretary of labor strengthened our beliefs that this administration is antiworker."
Kathleen Harrington, a labor department spokeswoman, said: "She was not insulting. She was not aggressive. She stood up for what she believed."
For months, labor's dismay with the Bush administration has been growing, but at the three-day meeting here, this dismay seemed to explode into bitterness and anger, with the labor federation vowing to do its utmost to defeat Mr. Bush in next year's elections.
Labor leaders have been angered by many administration actions, including its backing the repeal of a new ergonomics law and its stripping 60,000 airport screeners of the right to join a union.
Mr. Buffenbarger, the machinists' president, said he was taken aback when Ms. Chao, in responding to his question, read details of seven cases of financial malfeasance by officials from his union. 
"I felt a little appalled that a labor secretary would come to the meeting prepared to attack the labor movement," he said. "She came prepared with her book of sins. It's like Satan at the gates of hell."
Mr. Buffenbarger pointed out that the union's own audits and internal investigations led to the criminal cases against the machinist officials.
Labor Department officials acknowledged today that Ms. Chao had a dossier describing crimes committed by officials in many of the 65 unions that are part of the A.F.L.-C.I.O. Department officials said the dossier helped buttress the administration's case to require greater financial disclosure by unions. Under the proposed regulations, unions would have to detail how much money they spend each year on politics, lobbying, organizing and strike benefits and would have to itemize all expenditures above either a $2,000 or $5,000 threshold.
Responding to labor's criticisms, Ms. Chao said: "As we talk about transparency and corporate malfeasance, organized labor has been in the forefront of asking for transparency. I would think that transparency is an issue they'd want to talk about in their own organizations as well."
Ms. Chao said the tighter regulations would deter malfeasance by union officials, but union leaders said existing disclosure regulations already do that.
She said they would impose only a small burden, perhaps requiring only 50 hours for a union to complete.
Many union leaders view the proposed regulations as retaliatory and say the disclosure requirements will call for tens of thousands of hours of work and perhaps millions of dollars because so many expenditures would have to be tracked and reported.
"Clearly everyone was chagrined by the secretary's presentation," said Sandra Feldman, president of the American Federation of Teachers. "We're facing rising health care costs, layoffs, companies going bankrupt, people losing retiree benefits. And it's upsetting that she had this huge book, and that was the main thing she came here with. That's not to say we don't want every union to be open and transparent and clean."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Labor Secretary Elaine L. Chao's remarks annoyed James P. Hoffa, below, the Teamsters president. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: The Accountable Mayor Bloomberg 

 BODY:
Michael Bloomberg, the mayor who is reinventing education in New York City, graded himself a while back. In his first 13 months in office, he said, 80 percent of his 380 campaign promises had been met, were on their way to fulfillment or had at least made it to the drawing board. That, he said good-naturedly, deserved A+. Even allowing for grade inflation, his effort to hold himself accountable is commendable. It also sets a useful precedent. 
 His list was nothing if not comprehensive. One item promised to "track city utilities using modern technology." That one is in the category "launched." Many higher-profile matters, including gaining control of the schools, banning smoking in restaurants and bars and continuing the crackdown on crime, have been entered in the "done" column. Some vows, like not raising taxes or borrowing money, were nullified by the fiscal crisis. 
 While polls continue to show his popularity lagging, especially among his old conservative base, this rookie politician keeps daring the public to take his measure. Right now, many seem to be giving him an F in tax hikes. But the report card might include other categories. For instance:
Social skills: Usually plays well with others, sometimes too well. Gov. George Pataki did little to help the city close its $3 billion deficit. But in public at least, Mr. Bloomberg has not fought back.
Work space: Clean, orderly, open and smoke-free.
Attendance: Always on the job, but disappears on weekends, sometimes re-emerging with a George-Hamiltonish glow.
Creativity: High marks here, from his initiative to increase housing units by tens of thousands to his plan to improve delivery of services through a special 311 line, both getting under way.
Attitude: Optimistic even in dire straits. The mayor keeps selling New York: He won the 2004 Republican National Convention and has New York in the running to be host to the 2012 Olympic Games.
Areas for improvement: Mr. Bloomberg has been trying to demonstrate more empathy for everyday New Yorkers, but he'll have to work harder at that. Sometimes it's the things that can't be measured that mean the most.

HEADLINE: Romney's Campus Plan Would Cut Deficit, and a Political Foe 

BYLINE:  By FOX BUTTERFIELD 

DATELINE: BOSTON, Feb. 26 

 BODY:
Gov. Mitt Romney today proposed to reorganize the University of Massachusetts as part of his plan to cut the state's huge budget deficit. In the process, he would eliminate the $309,000-a-year job of the school's president, William M. Bulger.
 Mr. Romney, a Republican, proposed the plan as a way to save $150 million and at least partly privatize the state university system. Under the plan, the university's main campus at Amherst would become largely independent, depending on increased tuition instead of state aid. The other 4 campuses along with 9 state colleges and 15 community colleges would be restructured into seven regional clusters. 
 But it also happens that Mr. Bulger is a Democrat from South Boston and one of the most powerful political figures in Massachusetts who served for 17 years as president of the state Senate. 
The proposal is expected to set off a major battle with the Legislature, which would decide whether to approve the plan and which is heavily controlled by Democrats, many of them still loyal to Mr. Bulger.
Some students of Boston were as interested in the politics as the educational restructuring. "This is a classic battle of the old Boston versus the new Boston," said Thomas H. O'Connor, a professor of history at Boston College.
"It's the Irish versus the WASP, a culture of a government that spends versus a government that cuts, and it involves a prevailing view in South Boston that the authorities are out to shaft Bulger."
Shawn Feddeman, a spokeswoman for Governor Romney, said the idea to trim Mr. Bulger's job "has nothing to do" with Mr. Bulger himself. "It would be the same if the president was John Doe or Jane Smith," Ms. Feddeman said.
Governor Romney's proposal says reorganizing the University of Massachusetts campuses, state colleges and community colleges into regional clusters would save taxpayer dollars through joint purchasing arrangements and by increasing tuition and fees. Under the plan, the reorganized schools would also focus more heavily on vocational education to help spur business development in the state.
Ms. Feddeman said another benefit of the reorganization would be the end of a need for a president of the overall University of Massachusetts and for the president's large office in downtown Boston, where Mr. Bulger has a staff of 68 people at a cost of $14 million.
"The governor would like to eliminate the need for that extra layer of bureaucracy," Ms. Feddeman said. The president and his staff would be replaced by a new executive office of education. The leaders of the regional universities would be elevated from the title of chancellor to president.
Professor O'Connor, the author of "The Boston Irish: A Political History," and many political figures here say they believe the reorganization plan also reflects a personal clash between Governor Romney and Mr. Bulger, though aides to both men denied it today.
Last December, Mr. Romney suggested that Mr. Bulger, 68, had a civic duty to "respect" Congress by testifying before a Congressional committee investigating his brother, James Bulger, 73, known as Whitey, who was the leader of Boston's most notorious organized crime gang. Whitey Bulger has been a fugitive since 1995, when he fled after a federal grand jury indicted him for racketeering and murder. He is on the Federal Bureau of Investigation's Most Wanted List. 
But William Bulger refused to answer questions by the House Government Reform Committee, citing his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination. Mr. Bulger was also reported to have told a federal grand jury investigating how his brother corrupted F.B.I. agents in Boston that he had "an honest loyalty" to his brother and felt no "obligation to help everyone to catch him."
Mr. Romney was said by aides at the time to be quietly furious about the president of the state university thumbing his nose at law enforcement. The governor could not directly remove Mr. Bulger, since he has been supported by the university's board of trustees and has a contract that runs until 2007.
But now, with his plan, Governor Romney may have found a way. 
Massachusetts faces a $3 billion budget deficit, and during last fall's election Mr. Romney pledged not to raise taxes to close the deficit.
A spokesman for the University of Massachusetts, Robert P. Connolly, said school officials were still trying to understand the details of Mr. Romney's plan.
Mr. Connolly said it appeared that Mr. Romney hoped to reduce state aid to higher education in Massachusetts by $300 million a year, largely by raising tuition and fees for students. In addition, he said, it would mean layoffs of 1,400 of the 13,000 employees just at the five university campuses.
Asked about Mr. Bulger's reaction to Mr. Romney's plan, Mr. Connolly said it was "one of great concern for the University of Massachusetts."
"He really believes dismantling the university is not in the best interest of the commonwealth," Mr. Connolly said.
But Mr. Connolly said it was not correct to portray Mr. Romney's proposal to eliminate Mr. Bulger's job as a personal or political attack. 
State Senator David Magnani, a Democrat who is vice chairman of the Senate education committee, said, "Everything the governor proposes will be taken seriously, because the truth of the matter is that we are facing an enormous deficit."
"But having said that," Senator Magnani added, "we need to look closely at whether the savings the governor proposes are real and whether closing the president's office saves money."
In fact, Senator Magnani said, Mr. Bulger has brought in more money for the university than his office costs by attracting new federal and private research dollars and keeping good relations with the Legislature, which has preserved the school's budget in hard economic times.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. Mitt Romney of Massachusetts gave a partial breakdown of state spending yesterday in Boston as he discussed budget cuts. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: HOW IT WORKS; 
From a White House Roof, Solar Power Proclaims Gains

BYLINE:  By LISA GUERNSEY 

 BODY:
THE White House may be occupied by an administration steeped in the oil industry, but solar power has quietly become part of the landscape at 1600 Pennsylvania Avenue.
 Since September, a grid of 167 solar panels on the roof of a maintenance shed has been delivering electricity to the White House grounds. Another solar installation has been helping to provide hot water. Yet another has been keeping the water warm in the presidential pool. 
James Doherty, an architect for the National Park Service, decided to install the systems a few years ago. It was time to replace the roof on what is affectionately called the Pony Shed, a maintenance building that replaced the stable that once housed Macaroni, a pony owned by President John F. Kennedy's daughter, Caroline. 
 The Park Service, which is responsible for the building, had already mandated that any refurbishments of its facilities should include environmentally friendly design where possible. 
 "We thought if we were able to reduce our energy consumption, that was a positive step forward," Mr. Doherty said.
 He installed solar panels, called photovoltaic or PV modules, along with a new copper roof.
 The panels are made up of thin wafers of silicon. When light photons hit the silicon atoms, electrons break free, creating a direct current. An inverter turns the electricity into an alternating current that can be fed into the White House power grid.
 The thermal water systems are installed on the roof too, but they work differently. Heat from sunlight is absorbed by water flowing behind specially formulated black absorber plates. The water is warmed directly by the sun without the need for a traditional water heater. 
 To some, the word "solar" evokes images of the 1970's, and the flashbacks aren't pretty. Many people remember leaky roofs and maintenance problems that came with solar water-heating systems. The problem with photovoltaic modules, which became commercially available in the early 1980's, was their price tag. They were initially so expensive that the electricity cost 100 times as much as power from conventional sources.
 "Today we are outgrowing that reputation," said Mark Farber, the chief executive of Evergreen Solar, a company in Marlboro, Mass., that supplied the solar roof modules. 
 Mr. Farber said that mass production and improved manufacturing techniques had pushed prices down, and estimated that photovoltaic technology now costs one and a half to four times as much as conventional power sources when costs are spread over time. But the cost can be lower than that of electricity from conventional sources in areas with high power bills and government subsidies for the use of alternative energy. 
 Glenn Hamer, executive director of the Solar Energy Industries Association, said that the leaky water heaters that tainted the industry had been driven from the market. 
 A slow but steady resurgence of solar technology has taken place in the last two decades. In 1982, the year the government's Energy Information Administration started tracking shipments of PV modules, about 7,000 peak kilowatts (the measure used by photovoltaic companies) were shipped to purchasers in the United States. In 2001, about five times that many -- 36,000 -- were shipped. 
 Across the country, and particularly in California, where energy costs have spiked, more solar roofs are being installed each year. Some are generating a surplus of electricity that can be sold to the electric companies. 
 "It's starting to get very exciting," said Steven J. Strong, the solar designer who guided the White House project and whose company, Solar Design Associates, has been advocating the technology since the 1970's. "We're busier than ever."
 At the same time, solar thermal systems for heating water have become almost commonplace for new pools, said Maureen McIntyre, editor of Solar Today, a monthly magazine. "We now have solar hot water systems that are mature and affordably priced and make sense," she said.
 Still, solar power makes up only 1 percent of the total energy generated in the United States, according to Mr. Hamer of the industries association. 
 Many environmentalists do not expect a federal focus on solar power anytime soon, given that President Bush and many members of his team worked in the oil industry.
 In fact, the announcement that solar technology had been installed at the White House came not from the government but from the solar industry, and only after the equipment had been in place for four months. The lack of fanfare may be because the scale of the project was small: the amount of electricity generated so far is minuscule. In the first four months, the panels generated about 1,100 kilowatt-hours of power, Mr. Doherty said. 
 Given that other institutions in the area pay 4.9 cents per kilowatt-hour in the winter, that translates into $54 in savings. Of course, that calculation does not take into account the cost of installing the system, which was $75,000 -- higher than usual for such a project because of the logistics of doing construction at the White House. (The cost of installing the two hot-water systems was $25,000.)
 Mr. Doherty said he expected the panels to yield more savings in the summer months, when traditional energy costs are higher and the PV modules capture more light. He stressed that the installation was a long-term investment. "We know it will pay for itself," he said. 

GRAPHIC: Drawings (Frank O'Connell/The New York Times) Chart: "Not Your Typical Roofing Job"Solar energy may not yet be as economical as power from conventional sources, but the technology has had a resurgence in recent years. Among the latest customers is the White House, where several solar systems, including a photovoltaic one, have been installed. THE INSTALLATIONBruce Newell of Evergreen Solar installing solarcell panels on the roof of a maintenance shed at the southwest corner of the White House grounds. THE PANELSEach panel contains 36 cells connected by conductive strips or bus bars. THE CELLSEach cell consists of two layers of doped silicon n-type, which has an excess negative charge, and p-type, with an excess positive charge. Sunlight hitting the silicon frees electrons, creating current. CONVERTING ENERGYIn a solar cell, the energy of light photons frees up electrons, which leave the cell, creating electricity. The sandwich of two types of silicon creates a diode, which allows electrons to flow in only one direction between the two layers. Photons striking atoms in the cell create free electrons , which migrate to one layer, and electron holes -- the absence of one electron -- which migrate to the other. USING THE POWERElectricity from a solar cell is converted to alternating current by an inverter, and then flows into the house wiring or back out into the electrical grid. (Sources: Evergreen Solar; Solar Energy Industries Association)

Bush Ties Iraq Success To Peace in Middle East
President Bush, under pressure from European and Arab countries, declared that removing Saddam Hussein from power would bring stability to the Middle East and could set the stage for peace between Israel and a "truly Democratic" Palestinian state. A1

HEADLINE: Voters Say Why Mayor Is Slipping In Popularity 

BYLINE:  By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
At the mention of Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg's name, Barbara Carr's arm moved furiously up and down, as if she were trying to wave away the odor of three-day-old cod.
 "Please, no, I can't stand him!" said Ms. Carr, turning her sunglassed gaze toward a case of cream pies and zeppoles in Alfonso's Pastry Shoppe, a popular bakery in the Meier's Corner neighborhood of Staten Island. 
 Ms. Carr, 45, cast her vote for Mr. Bloomberg with a majority of Staten Island voters in the 2001 election. But the mayor's decision to raise property taxes 18.5 percent, his antismoking legislation and his scant appearances in her borough have since turned her against him. 
"At that time I thought he was going to do things for us," Ms. Carr said. "But he has come down so hard on us, it's absurd. To me, New York City is his little play toy that he sits back and plays with and then jets back to one of his islands as he watches us fall into chaos."
To be fair, Mr. Bloomberg does not own any islands, although he does have a nice house in Bermuda. And neighborhoods like Ms. Carr's, Grant City, more or less enjoy the same level of services as a year ago, according to the latest Mayor's Management Report. Crime in Staten Island also fell 11.7 percent from 2001 to 2002 -- hardly chaos.
But Ms. Carr's profound distaste for the mayor represents a potentially alarming erosion of support for Mr. Bloomberg among New Yorkers who made him their mayor after Rudolph W. Giuliani left: the conservative, property-owning, Giuliani-loving New Yorkers who helped him squeak to victory. It explains more than anything else why a New York Times poll taken in January found that more than half of New Yorkers disapprove of his handling of the job even as he lost little ground in Manhattan.
Yesterday, a Quinnipiac University poll put the mayor's approval rating at 48 percent citywide.
Mr. Bloomberg won 45 percent of the vote in Manhattan, and yesterday's poll had him gaining in that borough. He is fading fast, however, among his key constituents. Mr. Bloomberg won 76 percent of the vote in Staten Island, but a majority of those residents now do not approve of his job performance, according to the Times poll.
In Queens, the other borough Mr. Bloomberg narrowly won (he took 54 percent of the vote there), only 31 percent approve of him now. Mr. Bloomberg gained only 41 percent of the Bronx and 44 percent of the Brooklyn vote, and his approval rating has sunk to 21 percent and 29 percent respectively in those boroughs.
Much of the erosion of support is among voters who were most affected by the property tax increase and, it would seem, least support his anti-smoking policies, based on interviews with those polled and with other New Yorkers. They are the people who least appreciate hearing about tolls on East River bridges, after a recent hefty increase in parking fines.
"He is obviously facing different problems than Giuliani," said Marco Ferrante, an auditor from Westerleigh who stopped into Alfonso's on Sunday (apple pie and a pound of cookies). "But I disagree with the way his only solution is to blindly raise taxes. I think he needs to cut services, because there are plenty of services that New York City provides that I could do without." 
Mr. Bloomberg and his aides dismiss polls as little more than snapshots taken at a bad moment during the party. His aides insist that, at the end of the day, New Yorkers will favorably view Mr. Bloomberg's accomplishments and get past their short-term personal pain.
"Unlike a senator or a congressman," said William T. Cunningham, the mayor's communication director, "mayors make daily decisions, and the people evaluate you based on those decisions."
But clearly, Mr. Bloomberg must either regain his base or make significant inroads among traditionally Democratic voters if he hopes to win the second term he claims to want. 
"When you lose your base, that is not a good sign," said Philip J. Friedman, a political consultant in Manhattan. "He is not doing a good job at conveying a sense of connection with the working people in the city, particularly among those who voted for him based on the endorsement of Giuliani -- outer-borough homeowners, Irish and Italian Catholics, people in small businesses. Among people like that, people who voted for him based on that, there is a significant level of disappointment."
Much of the unhappiness has to do with the tax increase, although even disenchanted voters acknowledge that the economic situation Mr. Bloomberg inherited was worse than anything Mr. Giuliani ever faced. But for many who once supported him, there is a feeling that he is piling on.
"I am 48 years old, my mom is 73 and we've lived in New York all our lives," Robin Leckow, who lives in Whitestone, Queens, wrote in a recent e-mail message to a reporter. Ms. Leckow said she is a smoker, like her mother, whose social life revolves around bingo. The new smoking law prohibiting smoking in bingo parlors has ruined that, she said. "She won't get out of the house now, or see her friends," Ms. Leckow said.
"The mayor has completely priced us out of buying our cigarettes in New York," added Ms. Leckow, who voted for Mr. Bloomberg. "So once a month we drive out of state and buy cartons for our own use. Now he wants to criminalize this, and hunt me down for taxes.
"To add insult to injury," she went on, "my one enjoyment on Saturday is visiting the Poppenhusen branch of the Queens Borough Public Library. Since I work during the week, I can't get there but Saturday. Thank the mayor for me next time you see him for canceling Saturday service there."
Educational policy has also threatened Mr. Bloomberg's support. His attempt to remake the city's public schools was expected to draw fire in minority neighborhoods, where the former centralized system was most popular. But it has also attracted controversy in the city's white ethnic enclaves.
"The educational issue is the most important one to my constituents," said State Senator Frank Padavan, a Republican from Queens, who, along with four other Republican legislators, has objected to the Department of Education's plan to replace 32 district superintendents with 10 regional superintendents. "This doesn't just affect children, which is paramount, but also the property values of homes. Districts 25 and 26 are high-performing districts, and they don't want to see that change."
Mr. Bloomberg has also been criticized for being remote. As if in response, he has spent more weekends visiting churches and attending evening events around the city, although usually in Democratic strongholds like minority neighborhoods in Brooklyn and the Bronx, not in Staten Island and eastern Queens.
Most political consultants said it is far too early for Mr. Bloomberg to be written off, and some noted New York Democrats' recent tendency to form a circular firing squad at election time, leaving Mr. Bloomberg open for another win. But the mayor has a plan to make this entire issue irrelevant -- he is pushing for nonpartisan municipal elections.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg at a recent groundbreaking in Staten Island, where his support has lagged. (Mary DiBiase Blaich for The New York Times)(pg. B4)

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY; 
F.C.C. Members Defend Proposals on Bell Companies

BYLINE:  By JENNIFER 8. LEE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 26 

 BODY:
The five members of the Federal Communications Commission defended their new telephone and broadband policy in front of a Congressional hearing today, but they conceded that their compromise proposal, which requires the regional Bell companies to continue to share their phone lines with competitors, left no one happy and was not certain to pump up the flailing telecommunications sector in the near term.
 The sharp divisions among the commissioners were aired in their first public appearance together since the widely anticipated vote last week for the new regulations. The regulations passed on a 3-to-2 vote. The commission members appeared before a panel of the House Committee on Energy and Commerce. Most of the lawmakers' questions, as well as some anger, were directed at the F.C.C. chairman, Michael K. Powell, and a commissioner, Kevin J. Martin, a Republican who had brokered a compromise with the two Democratic members of the commission. 
 The critics of the compromise included some congressmen who have been among the most outspoken advocates for the so-called Baby Bells -- Verizon, SBC, BellSouth and Qwest Communications International. 
The Bells, which have been lobbying for deregulatory relief for years, had been pushing for new rules that would free them from sharing both their telephone and broadband operations with competitors.
Instead, the commission freed them from sharing broadband lines but forced the Baby Bells to continue to share their telephone lines until state regulators determine that there is enough local competition to permit deregulation.
"I believe the order we adopted last week achieves a principled, balanced approach," said Mr. Martin, who has close ties to the White House. 
After the ruling, many of the local phone companies announced that they would not be making new investments in high-speed services as a result of getting only half of what they wanted from the ruling. But Mr. Powell dismissed the Baby Bells' reaction, saying, "Here is a lot of crying crybaby reaction to the decision." He said he thought that the announcements were more like "public affairs reactions" than like reasoned management decisions. Mr. Powell also said he was getting tired of the "passion play between billion-dollar self-interested actors."
The committee, which is generally sympathetic to the Baby Bells, also criticized the local phone companies for their failure to invest. Several congressmen noted that the Baby Bells had received a lot of what they had asked for since the 1996 Telecommunications Act but were still not making the investments that they had promised they would make.
The sense of impatience among House members is notable because the House is often seen as being more favorable to the Baby Bells than the Senate. There are a large number of telecommunications jobs in local districts, and the industry has engaged in intense lobbying over the last several years. 
Last year, after much lobbying, the House passed the Tauzin-Dingell broadband deregulation bill, which would have freed the Bells from some of the line-sharing requirements. While the bill was halted in the Senate by Senator Ernest Hollings, last week's F.C.C. ruling essentially accomplishes a lot of what was in the Tauzin-Dingell bill and more. The commission's ruling means that the Bells are not obligated to make new investments in infrastructure, as they were under Tauzin-Dingell. 
But the congressmen and the commissioners all noted that the continuing regulatory uncertainty that would come with state-by-state evaluations on local phone service would probably stall telecommunications investment as the rules made their way through court challenges.
A number of congressmen were overtly hostile on this point, including Representative Billy Tauzin, the Louisiana Republican who is the chairman of the House Committee on Energy and Commerce. Mr. Tauzin was critical of the regulatory role of the states. He argued that the ruling would result in 51 local procedures with 51 lawsuits and 12 different appeals courts, "ending up at the Supreme Court that ordered the deregulation in the first place."
But in a letter sent to Mr. Powell in June 2002, Mr. Tauzin himself wrote: "The commission must evaluate the rationale for requiring the unbundling of a network element based upon specific geographic and class-of-customer characteristics of individual markets across the nation. Uniform, national rules do not accurately reflect the state of competition and the unique economic characteristics of individual markets."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Michael K. Powell, left, is chairman of the Federal Communications Commission. Kevin J. Martin is a Republican commissioner. (Doug Mills/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: HAVENS; 
Weekender Westport, Conn.

BYLINE:  By JOANNE KAUFMAN 

 BODY:
AT a recent meeting of the Westport Representative Town Meeting, the local governing body, two members put forth a resolution on Iraq: "Let the United Nations work it out and exhaust diplomatic and economic means to avoid war." Meanwhile, another member and some townspeople asked for a review of the regulations about the gamboling rights of dogs without leashes on Compo Beach. 
 The Iraq resolution was withdrawn after recent Security Council deliberations. A section of the beach from the bathhouse south, it was finally decided, would be cordoned off from fall to spring to accommodate dogs yearning to run free. 
 "The whole thing generated a lot of controversy," said Gordon Joseloff, the town meeting's moderator (and, in the interest of full disclosure, the writer's brother-in-law). "Some saw it as an attempt to limit the beach to dogs from Westport, that it was reminiscent of how they were trying to restrict beach access in Greenwich, and that it seemed unwelcoming." 
Inclusiveness, whether in the matter of quadrupeds or bipeds or international affairs, the buttoned-down or the unbuttoned, is what Westport is all about. It seems that everyone is on a committee, ad hoc or otherwise. Peter DeVries, the novelist and a Westport resident, was smack on target when he wrote in a 1950 Holiday magazine article that the town was so heterogeneous "that a crazy quilt would do as a metaphor for it, provided you mean one in which there is not even any unity of fabrics."
Another resident, the actress Joanne Woodward, described the town as "eclectic and cosmopolitan" in the foreword to the book "Westport, Connecticut" by Woody Klein (Greenwood Press, 2000). "It's become a Mecca for those who are traditionally untraditional," she wrote.
Italians came to build railroads in the 19th century and were followed by Germans and Eastern Europeans, and they all formed friendships with the indigenous Yankee farmers.
"Westport was much more open to this influx than other towns on Connecticut's Gold Coast," said Mr. Klein, press secretary to Mayor John V. Lindsay during Mr. Lindsay's first term and a Westport weekender who became a full-time resident.
In the 20th century, the town earned a reputation as an artists' colony. F. Scott Fitzgerald, with his wife, Zelda, in tow, wrote part of "The Great Gatsby" there. Elizabeth Taylor was a fixture during her marriage to Mike Todd. Tallulah Bankhead, idiosyncratic even for an actress and a Southerner, was a resident, as were Charles and Anne Morrow Lindbergh, Bette Davis, Helen Keller, Martha Raye, Neil Sedaka and Rod Serling.
Possibly because of its long roster of celebrities, Westport manages to keep its collective cool when a big name swings into view. "There's a code of behavior that everyone understands," Mr. Klein said. "Paul Newman can walk down the street and no one will look into his blue eyes. Well-known people come here and they know they'll be left alone." 

The Scene
Westport is a town for all seasons and for all interests. In the winter there are skating and cross-country skiing at the 191-acre Longshore Country Club, bought by the town in 1960, which also has a golf course, three swimming pools, tennis courts and a sailing school. Westporters buy annual "handpasses" ($35 for a full-time adult resident and $145 for a "nonresident taxpayer," meaning a weekend-home owner) for access to Longshore's attractions; golfers still must pay greens fees ($10 to $16 for handpass holders).
Residents have their choice of sand -- Compo Beach on Long Island Sound, Old Mill Beach and Burying Hill Beach. Kayaks ply the waters of Old Mill Pond and the Saugatuck River, which runs close enough to the local Starbucks that you can moor your canoe and take a latte break. Those who prefer their boats with sails or motors have the Minute Man and Cedar Point Yacht Clubs.
On land, there are the Westport Country Playhouse, where the Broadway production of "Our Town," starring Paul Newman (Ms. Woodward's husband) originated; the White Barn Theater; the Levitt Pavilion, a band shell that is the site of summer concerts; the Westport Arts Center; and the Westport Library, with reading rooms that overlook the Saugatuck River. On most Saturdays there is a story time for children, and on Sundays there are concerts, talks by authors and movies. On Sunday, as part of Black History Month, Ossie Davis is scheduled to be on hand for a screening of "A Raisin in the Sun," the 1961 film starring Sidney Poitier and Ruby Dee.
The lines are long and the doughnuts first rate at Coffee An'. Customers, according to Elias Vlandis, the owner, include the radio personality Don Imus and Bill Clinton, who, Mr. Vlandis said, received occasional doughnut shipments at the White House.
A few blocks away, at Westport Pizza, one of the few businesses left on Main Street that are not part of a chain, families lay claim to the tables in the rear while teenagers grab the real estate out front. Young patrons are offered lollipops from a Tupperware tub. The welcoming atmosphere may explain the strong, if unsuccessful, showing by the owner, Joseph Mioli, when he ran for the state's General Assembly a few years back.
When John and Lynn Shanahan, parents of three young children, began looking for a weekend house, their first priority was a town with an easy commute from New York. Westport had that and more, said Mr. Shanahan, 43, a commodities trader turned teacher.
"It was the first town on the sound that was pretty self-sufficient," he said. "It wasn't a bedroom community."
He added, "There's a down-home atmosphere and a real town spirit."
Never is that spirit more apparent than during the Memorial Day parade. So what if the participants -- Boy Scouts, Girl Scouts, Little Leaguers, firemen, emergency medical service personnel, the League of Women Voters in stars and stripes -- frequently outnumber the spectators. "I grew up here and can remember marching in the parade as a Girl Scout," said Diane Farrell, Westport's first selectwoman. "It's a thrill to see the festivities. Norman Rockwell couldn't paint a more poignant picture of hometown America."

Pros
"You get a full holiday experience here, and it's only an hour away from the city," said Tom Seligson, an executive producer with CBS News who grew up in Westport and returned to raise a family. "There's a party at the beach all summer long, and it's not exclusionary. All you have to do is show up and you're part of it."
While the mom and pop store is all but a thing of the past on Main Street, there is still a small-town feeling in the shopping area, and odds are good, Mr. Seligson said, that the druggist will know your name.
"You get a lot of bang for your tax dollars," said Mr. Joseloff, the town meeting moderator. "They're rising," he said of taxes, "but still low compared to Westchester or Long Island."

Cons
People think twice before venturing downtown on weekends -- and even weekdays -- because traffic and parking are daunting. Parking is also tight at Compo Beach in the summer. 
Main Street sometimes seems like a mall, one with a pretty tough crowd. The town seems to attract competitive Type A people and, as one woman said, "Everyone is so thin."
"Westport has become a bit of Beverly Hills," said another resident, who asked that his name not be attached to comments that might offend some of his neighbors. "It's caught that disease. There is a tendency to show off your wealth with a big home and a big car." 

Real Estate Market
"The common misconception is that prices are going down," said Beverly Walsh, sales manager of the William Raveis Real Estate office in Westport (203-227-4343; www.raveis.com). Indeed, a home priced last year at $700,000 would have a sticker price of $770,000 today, a 10 percent increase that has been a constant over the last few years. Ms. Walsh said that the market was not hot, but that "if a house is attractively priced, it gets multiple offers."
"What you get for your money in Westport," she added, "is far greater value than in Greenwich or Darien or New Canaan for about the same commute."
In some parts of town, particularly near Compo Beach, houses sit cheek by jowl. North of the Merritt Parkway, however, two-acre-plus parcels are common. Colonials are more abundant than anything else, but buyers can find antiques, contemporaries, Cape Cods and the occasional stone house. What they won't find are homes under $400,000 or many under $500,000. Under $600,000? "Well," Ms. Walsh said. "Right now I've got four."

LAY OF THE LAND
A Place Where Celebrities Can Relax

POPULATION -- 25,749.
SIZE -- 22 square miles.

NEIGHBORS -- Weekenders and full-time residents include executives in advertising and financial services, entrepreneurs, artists and writers. Paul Newman and Joanne Woodward, Marlo Thomas and Phil Donahue, Michael Bolton, and Don Imus live here.

MEDIAN HOUSE PRICE -- $805,000.

RECENT SALES -- A three-bedroom antique farmhouse on less than half an acre with a detached two-car garage sold in 28 days for $595,000. A four-bedroom contemporaryon one acre with a heated driveway and a new kitchen sold for $895,000, also after 28 days. A five-bedroom colonial with a pool on half an acre very close to town sold for $1.4 million in 45 days. A six-bedroom house built last year on one acre with a three-car attached garage, professional kitchen and media room sold for $1.96 million after 108 days.

DISTANCE FROM NEW YORK -- 42 miles.
TRAVEL TIME -- An hour in light traffic; an hour by Metro North Railroad, which runs more than 30 trains in each direction on weekdays and more than 20 each way on Saturdays and Sundays.

GETTING THERE -- Take the Merritt Parkway to Exit 41 or 42.

WHILE YOU'RE LOOKING -- Stay at the Inn at Longshore (260 South Compo Road, 203-226-3316); its 12 rooms and suites are $160 to $235 on weekends. A Continental breakfast is included. Or try the Westport Inn (1595 Post Road East, 203-259-5236), which has 116 rooms, an indoor pool and Continental breakfast. Weekend rates are $105 to $130 until March 1, when they jump to $154 to $340.
