PUB-DATE: February 28, 2003
 Late Edition - Final 

SECTION: Section E; Part 2; Column 1; Leisure/Weekend Desk; Pg. 46

LENGTH: 4689 words

HEADLINE: ART GUIDE 

 BODY:
 A selective listing by critics of The Times of new or noteworthy art, design and photography exhibitions at New York museums and art galleries this weekend. At many museums, children under 12 and museum members are admitted free. Addresses, unless otherwise noted, are in Manhattan. Most galleries are closed on Sundays and Mondays, but hours vary and should be checked by telephone. Gallery admission is free. * denotes a highly recommended show.

Art Fairs

"WORKS ON PAPER," Seventh Regiment Armory, Park Avenue at 67th Street, (212) 777-5218 (through Sunday). Hours: Today, noon to 8 p.m.; tomorrow, 11 a.m. to 7 p.m.; Sunday, noon to 7 p.m. Admission: $15. The least expensive and most generously receptive of art-making surfaces has its moment. Eighty-nine dealers from Europe and North America are offering drawings, watercolors, prints, photographs, posters and books. Visitors will find works by all the usual suspects, from old masters like Rembrandt and Durer, to Modernists like Picasso and Matisse, to young and emerging stars of the future. You may also discover all kinds of strange and arcane things from the more specialized fields of art history. Prices can be mind-boggling, or surprisingly reasonable. If you are thinking of starting an art collection but lack deep pockets, there is no better place to begin (Ken Johnson).

Museums

JO BAER, "The Minimalist Years, 1960-1975," Dia Center for the Arts, 548 West 22nd Street, Chelsea, (212) 989-5566 (through June 13). In the mid-1960's, Ms. Baer framed blazing white fields with black bands inlaid with strips of bright color and made some of modernism's most austere paintings. Mimicking picture frames and note paper while enforcing an intense perceptual focus, they are at once sardonically self-referential and romantic, and they form the high point of this small survey of her early years. Also on view are some tentative first steps and weaker later works, whose boxy canvases make their eccentric painted motifs look arbitrary and decorative. The sense of an artist running out of steam is corroborated by Ms. Baer's moving to Ireland in the late 1970's and working in a figurative style. Hours: Wednesdays through Sundays, noon to 6 p.m. Admission: $6; $3, children, students and 60+ (Roberta Smith).

* "MATTHEW BARNEY: THE CREMASTER CYCLE," Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum, 1071 Fifth Avenue, at 89th Street, (212) 423-3500 (through June 11). The most anticipated show by any American artist to emerge in a decade or more. "Cremaster," Mr. Barney's nine-year, five-film project, here for the first time presented whole, is an inspired benchmark of ambition, scope and forthright provocation for art in the new century. I recall Elizabeth Murray's describing how Philip Guston's crazy late works gave her heart to do her own mature paintings. Mr. Barney has brought to the art world a new freedom to explore narrative. He has introduced a new repertory of materials, gooey, strange, malleable and synthetic, which nobody thought of as germane before. Never mind that the films and many (not all) of his related sculptures, installations and photographs are very beautiful. The show, an installation itself, brings them together. The earliest "Cremaster" sculptures are film props, cobbled into installations that don't gel. But the latest are better: twin steel towers framed in plastic; a suite of abstract sculptures derived from automobile chassis. Vaseline and plastic combine with cement and steel -- pink and gray, rough and smooth, hard and soft -- the materials sustaining a narrative of formal eloquence. Hours: Saturdays through Wednesdays, 10 a.m. to 5:45 p.m; Fridays, 10 a.m. to 8 p.m. Admission: $12; $9 for students and 65+ (Michael Kimmelman).

* "MONTIEN BOONMA: THE TEMPLE OF THE MIND," Asia Society and Museum, 725 Park Avenue, at 70th Street, (212) 517-2742 (through May 11). The art of the Thai artist Montien Boonma (1953-2000) combines many influences, from Southeast Asian Buddhism to Arte Povera and Joseph Beuys. But his art, by turns witty and somber, is not primarily about hybrid identities; it is about how to forge a life of psychic balance in an unbalanced and unbalancing world. In part he did so through the use of repeated, resonant symbols: the monk's alms bowl, the temple bell. He also turned art into an embracing sensual experience by infusing his installations with healing fragrances. There's nobody like him and nothing in town like this modestly scaled survey show. Hours: Tuesdays through Sundays, 11 a.m. to 6 p.m.; Fridays to 9 p.m. Admission: $7; $5, students and 65+ (Holland Cotter).

* "CHALLENGE OF THE MODERN: AFRICAN-AMERICAN ARTISTS 1925-1945," Studio Museum in Harlem, 144 West 125th Street, (212) 864-4500 (through March 30). This ambitious show tries to demonstrate how Modernism affected the art of African-Americans differently from the way it did white artists. Among the elements were the belated engagement with African art; the image of the "New Negro," whose militant behavior broke from traditional conformity with white expectations; performance, sexuality and the black body, as exemplified by Paul Robeson, Josephine Baker and Joe Louis; and the great migration from the rural South to the urban North between 1913 and 1946. The more than 150 works range from subtle abstractions by Norman Lewis and Wifredo Lam to lively photographs of protest demonstrations and Harlem social life by James VanDerZee and Morgan and Marvin Smith. A stimulating and well-presented roundup, although some offerings that contribute to the show's thesis do not serve it well as artistic examples. Hours: Wednesdays through Fridays, and Sundays, noon to 6 p.m.; Saturdays, 10 a.m. to 6 p.m. Admission: $5; $3 for students and 62+; $1, children under 12 (Grace Glueck).

* "THE KIDS ARE ALRIGHT: PHOTOGRAPHS BY RYAN McGINLEY," Whitney Museum of American Art, 945 Madison Avenue, at 75th Street, (212) 570-3676 (through May 18). It isn't often that an artist makes his New York solo exhibition debut in the august galleries of the Whitney. But Mr. McGinley, 26, is doing so as part of the museum's "First Exposure" series, a showcase for new photography. He works in what might be described as a life-style genre, whose exemplars also include Nan Goldin, Wolfgang Tillmans and Larry Clark. The 20 large color pictures of Mr. McGinley's 20-something friends, lovers and fellow artists avoid the abjective grit of Mr. Clark's work and the noirish narcissicism of Ms. Goldin's. Instead, these are basically sunny, feel-good pictures, particularly interesting for an approach to sexuality that suggests intriguing socio-political shifts in gay self-presentation. Hours: Tuesdays through Thursdays, Saturdays and Sundays, 11 a.m. to 6 p.m.; Fridays, 1 to 9 p.m. Admission: $12; $9.50, students and 62+ (Cotter).

* "LEONARDO DA VINCI, MASTER DRAFTSMAN," Metropolitan Museum of Art, Fifth Avenue at 82nd Street, (212) 535-7710 (through March 30). This show has blockbuster written all over it. With 118 of Leonardo's drawings, it is the largest gathering of his work yet seen in America. The lending institutions are superstarry: the Uffizi, the Louvre, the Vatican, the Royal Library at Windsor Castle. And the Met has given it the imperial treatment: crimson walls, acres of space, a catalog as thick as as a couple of telephone books. Are the drawings, which represent the bulk of this artist's surviving work, worth all the fuss? In a word, totally. Individually, many are glorious, some are workmanlike, a few are plain weird. As a package, though -- as the data stream output of a single sensibility -- they are huge, and very alive. Hours: Sundays and Tuesdays through Thursdays, 9:30 a.m. to 5:30 p.m.; Fridays and Saturdays until 9 p.m. Admission: $12; $7, students and 65+ (Cotter).

* "MATISSE PICASSO," Museum of Modern Art, Queens, 33rd Street at Queens Boulevard, Long Island City, (212) 708-9400 (through May 19). If you're thinking of skipping it because of the crowds, that's not a good enough excuse. This is one for the ages: an eye-educating, often revelatory parlor game, with the fate of modern art as the stakes. The premise is a little dubious -- the two of them competing as if in stately isolation from everything else going on in art -- but the results look great. Choosing a winner is the sport of the event and the inevitable outcome of pitting titans mano a mano. Matisse comes out looking consistently good. To grasp 20th-century art, Picasso once said, "You ought to see side by side everything Matisse and I were doing." He was right about that. Hours: Thursdays through Mondays, 10 a.m. to 5 p.m; to 9 p.m. on Fridays and Saturdays. Admission to this exhibition: $20; $15.50, students and 65+; under 16 with an adult, free. Reserved tickets: Ticketmaster at (212) 307-5577; or toll free at (866) 879-6662, with Ticketmaster surcharges (Kimmelman).

* "THE QUILTS OF GEE'S BEND," Whitney Museum of American Art, 945 Madison Avenue, at 75th Street, (212) 570-3676 (through March 9). The most ebullient exhibition of the New York art season comes in the unlikely guise of a show of hand-stitched quilts from a remote, historically black community along the Alabama River. For generations the women of Gee's Bend have passed down an indigenous style of quilting geometric patterns out of old britches, cornmeal sacks, Sears corduroy swatches and hand-me-down leisure suits -- whatever happened to be around. Imagine Matisse and Klee arising not from rarefied Europe but from the soil of the rural South. The best designs, minimalist and spare, turn out to be some of the most miraculous works of modern art that America has produced. Hours and admission: See above (Kimmelman).

* "THOMAS STRUTH, 1977-2002," Metropolitan Museum of Art, Fifth Avenue at 82nd Street, (212) 535-7710 (through May 18). This midcareer retrospective is the right event for right now: serious, tradition-bound, circumspect about the world. Mr. Struth operates from a chilly peak, where the air is thin. But what results are obsessively meticulous photographs, some of them enormous, like mural paintings. They are hypnotic, silent, slightly claustrophobic, brainy and gorgeous, qualities that can seem in lively tension with one another. Art is a means of "locating yourself in your time," Mr. Struth has said. He has done series of streets, jungles, museums, portraits and church facades. Standing before his crowded museums and churches, we can become absorbed in the chaos of culture, sacred places made profane. In the jungle we acquiesce to the spiritual pleasure of solitude. The silence is calming. We may find ourselves in our time. Hours and admission: See above (Kimmelman).

"ANNE VALLAYER-COSTER: PAINTER TO THE COURT OF MARIE ANTOINETTE," Frick Collection, 1 East 70th Street, (212) 288-0700 (through March 23). Vallayer-Coster (1744-1818), the daughter of a Parisian jewlery dealer, had grand successes as a painter early on. At 26, she was elected a full member of the Royal Academy. A decade later, Marie Antoinette commissioned a portrait. But it was as a still-life painter that she was most valued, and will probably continue to be on the evidence of this attractive show. Its star entry, the large painting "Still Life With Seashells and Coral" (1769), is a tour de force: ghostly, sexy, glamorously bizarre. Hours: Tuesdays through Saturdays, 10 a.m. to 6 p.m.; Sundays, 1 to 6 p.m. Admission: $12; $5 for students; $8 for 62+ (Cotter).

* "VIDEO ACTS: SINGLE CHANNEL WORKS FROM THE COLLECTIONS OF PAMELA AND RICHARD KRAMLICH AND NEW ART TRUST," P.S. 1 Contemporary Art Center, 22-25 Jackson Avenue at 46th Street, Long Island City, Queens, (718) 784-2084 (through April 13). Wading through this sprawling survey of early single-channel video art is more edifying than enjoyable, but anyone interested in contemporary art should make the effort. Expertly installed with viewing efficiency in mind, it concentrates on works made mostly by lone artists experimenting behind closed studio doors in the 1970's, with special emphasis on the body-oriented efforts of Bruce Nauman, Vito Acconci, Joan Jonas and Marina Abramovic and Ulay. A handful of recent videotapes by Pipilotti Rist and others underscores the feeling that both the medium and its early pioneers have come a long way -- and there is, thankfully, no going back. Hours: Thursdays through Mondays, noon to 6 p.m. Admission: $5; $2 for students and 62+ (Smith).

Galleries: 57th Street

EDWIN DICKINSON, Babcock, 724 Fifth Avenue, (212) 767-1852 (through March 28). Dickinson was a modern romantic who drew with magical old-masterly refinement. A kind of melancholic ecstasy animates the modestly scaled paintings and drawings in this show. Pictures of blocky houses exude Hopperesque nostalgia. The image of a bench by the sea borders on the mystical. Drawings of old buildings in Europe and the inside of a greenhouse are like Proustian reveries (Johnson).

Galleries: Chelsea

ELSA D'ARRIGO, Elizabeth Harris, 529 West 20th Street, (212) 463-9666 (through March 8). These bounteous constructions, made with cloth, paint and thread, are composed of small units linked in honeycomb arrangements that suggest cellular growth. Cascading down a wall, flattened against one or rising from the floor, each is painted in subtly applied colors. Aside from suggesting nature's exuberant hand, the repetition of the units into an assembled mass is intended to invoke the idea of shrines with their accretions of objects. And it works: the earthy connects with the spiritual here (Glueck).

WENDELL GLADSTONE, "Four Seasons and Other Paintings," Kravets/Wehby, 521 West 21st Street, (212) 352-2238 (through March 15). Mr. Gladstone inventively practices the contemporary genre in which paint itself is treated as a near sculptural material. His medium-large pictures are strange, fairy-talelike scenes, involving dogs, birds and a dead deer in a shifty, primordial world. They look as if they were made by gluing onto raw canvas pieces of carefully cut out, variously textured and brightly colored paint. The possibilities for further development are exciting (Johnson).

ETHAN JACKSON, "Eden," Cornell DeWitt, 521 West 26th Street, (212) 695-6845 (through March 15). Tacked up on bulletin boards in general stores all over Vermont are snapshots of deer hunters proudly posing with the deer they killed. Mr. Jackson collects these pictures and here presents hundreds of digitally copied examples organized in grids. He has also incorporated images of dead deer into overwrought Surrealistic montages. It is the repulsive vernacular snaps, all different yet all weirdly similar, that captivate (Johnson).

PETER LOFTUS AND MEG SHIELDS, Fischbach, 210 11th Avenue, at 24th Street, (212) 759-2345 (through March 15). West meets East. In "Mendocino to Monterey" Mr. Loftus makes paintings of the rugged coast of California in which light, color and focus are amplified to near trippy effect; up close you discover a pleasing dry and brushy painterly application. Working in Provincetown and other East Coast locales, Ms. Shields makes small, squarish landscapes in which there is always a water tower. Titled "Landmarks," their smoothly painted naturalism, sensitivity to light and romantic moods recall the 19th-century Luminist painters (Johnson).

* INIGO MANGLANO-OVALLE, "Purgatory," Max Protetch, 511 West 22nd Street, (212) 633-6999 (through March 8). Possibly the most beautiful object to be found in a contemporary art gallery right now is Mr. Manglano-Ovalle's "Cloud Prototype No. 1," an elephantine, bulbous volume covered in satiny titanium leaf and suspended in midair. It is based on digital analysis of an actual thunderstorm cloud and is just one element of a grand scheme to realize a modern version of Dante's "Divine Comedy"(Johnson).

DONALD MOFFETT, "The Extravagant Vein," Marianne Boesky, 535 West 22nd Street, (212) 680-9889 (through March 15). Since the 1980's, Mr. Moffett has been making some of the quietest, subtlest political art around, and he continues to do so. The main part of this show is a series of impressionistic-looking landscapes of bridges and leafy glades. From a distance they have the flickering iridescence of Monet's Giverney paintings; in fact, they are films projected onto canvases. All the images are of the Rambles in Central Park, an area known both as a bird watching territory and as a cruising spot for gay sex. Mr. Moffett's pictures convey the tension of not knowing what will happen next, felt in different ways by birders and cruisers alike. A series of pencil drawings titled "Mr. Gay in the U.S.A" is installed in Boesky's second gallery (Cotter).

* JONATHAN PYLYPCHUK, Friedrich Petzel, 535 West 22nd Street, (212) 680-9467 (through March 1). Cobbled from wood, fur, rags, glue and paint, Mr. Pylypcuk's zany assemblages feature anthropomorphic animals in emotionally fraught situations. Two bird-like creatures with missing legs sit on wheeled palettes wondering, "Now How Will We Get Around?" A broken-legged cat watches a knife fight between another mangy feline and a blocky dwarf. A furry 10-foot-tall tree spewing resin into a fat bird's mouth is a nightmare of co-dependency (Johnson).

JAN STALLER, "Found Sculpture," Klotz/Sirmon, 511 West 25th Street, (212) 741-4764 (through March 29). A currently popular form of the photographic sublime is the big picture of an awesome industrial site. Mr. Staller's wide, glossy images of a vast field of plywood boxes, a knitting factory interior, a pile of giant logs with their ends painted blue and marked with red X's are not notably original, but they are formally impressive and romantically engaging (Johnson).

"TOO MUCH FLAVOR," Chambers Fine Art, 210 11th Avenue, near 24th Street, (212) 414-1169 (through March 8). This show of seven young Chinese artists is an edited, truncated version of a larger exhibition that appeared in Shanghai and Singapore. Its theme is the consumerist overload that has accompanied a market economy in China, swamping traditional Confucian ideals of a balance between abstemiousness and greed, and harmony between culture and nature (Cotter).

SUSAN WIDES, "Arachnoid," Kim Foster, 529 West 20th Street, (212) 229-0044 (through March 15). A photographer interested in the complicated ways we relate to nature here presents medium-large color pictures of little spiders in the midst of expansive, gossamer webs. The interest is less in the subject matter than in the subtle effects of light, reflection, color, pattern and shifty focus (Johnson).

Other Galleries

"CLAY WORKS: 20TH-CENTURY AMERICAN CERAMICS FROM THE EVERSON MUSEUM OF ART," UBS PaineWebber Art Gallery, 1285 Avenue of the Americas, at 51st Street, (212) 713-2885 (through March 28). Some 60 American ceramic works from the notable collection of the Everson Museum in Syracuse are displayed here, with the focus on the mid- to late 20th century. The show touches on major trends and ceramists, from turn-of-the-century art pottery to innovators like Peter Voulkos and Robert Arneson. A section on utilitarian pottery is cleverly counterpointed by one titled "Utility Trumped," showing works that play with the notion of use but are not really meant for such mundane purposes. If there is a weakness, it is the figural sculpture, which overall seems less substantial than the rest (Glueck).

* CHARLES LeDRAY, Sperone Westwater, 415 West 13th Street, West Village, (212) 999-7337 (through March 1). A sculptor known for his miniature men's suits here offers an outfit for a pint-sized mechanic. It has green trousers, a heavy duty jacket, a leather tool belt with little tools and leather gloves, all lovingly handmade. Also on view is a collection of thousands of tiny ceramic pots, each individually formed and glazed; and a black velvet "jewelry display" that has the haunted air of a graveyard (Johnson).

* "THE PARK AVENUE CUBISTS: GALLATIN, MORRIS, FRELINGHUYSEN AND SHAW," Grey Art Gallery, New York University, 100 Washington Square East, Greenwich Village, (212) 988-6780 (through March 29). Fueled by family money, four 20th-century artists -- Albert E. Gallatin, Charles G. Shaw, Suzy Frelinghuysen and George L. K. Morris -- enjoyed stylish, Cole Porteresque lives. Colleagues dubbed them the Park Avenue Cubists, but they were also dedicated artists. Although each hewed to an individual style and imagery, they shared a belief that they were the heirs and custodians of European modernism, with Picasso, Braque, Gris and Leger as their models. They recycled Cubist motifs and structures freely long before appropriation became accepted in the art world. Their art got lost for a while in the tide of Abstract Expressionism, but it was too good to be forgotten; in today's retro climate, where originality is not such an issue, their energies shine forth (Glueck).

CLAUDIA SCHMACKE, Plane Space, 102 Charles Street, Greenwich Village, (917) 606-1268 (through March 23). Mesmerizing and somehow slightly comical, this German artist's installation consists of beachball-size snarls of transparent tubing connected to small electrical pumps that circulate greenish-yellow liquid through the system. Ms. Schmacke also offers a series of diverting short videos, close-up, quasi-scientific recordings of watery phenomena like streams, waves, bubbles and whirlpools (Johnson).

"WERE YOU ALL RIGHT YESTERDAY?" White Columns, 320 West 13th Street, entrance on Horatio Street, West Village, (212) 924-4212 (through March 9). Libby McInnis's pair of giant puppets enacting a moment of unspeakable violence is the exception in this otherwise gently beguiling show. Rebecca Westcott makes sensitive portraits of young women; Ben Woodward paints chubby, hairy humanoids; and Kevin Christy, in gouache and ink, and Nils Karsten, in pencil, produce dreamy montages. Jeff Ladouceur's drawings suggest Walt Disney on a bad psychedelic trip and Taylor McKimens's suburban backyard installation features a glum man who looks like a refugee from an R. Crumb comic (Johnson).

Last Chance

LOIS DODD, "Windows and Doorways: Paintings of Three Decades," Alexandre Gallery, 41 East 57th Street, (212) 755-2828 (through tomorrow). This survey of work from 1971 through 1992 by a conservative yet uncommonly accomplished painter deals with her recurring motifs of doors and windows, viewed as passages to interior space as well as the outside world. Often preceding, then surviving, the human beings they serve, these simple passages celebrate time and memory as well as the here and now. Celebrating the richness of everyday life, these paintings touch on the transcendental (Glueck).

* "FACING THE MASK," Museum for African Art, third floor, 36-01 43rd Avenue, at 36th Street, Long Island City, Queens, (718) 784-7700 (through Sunday). The Museum for African Art, formerly of SoHo, makes the leap to a new home in Long Island City with a very beautiful if surveyish show, "Facing the Mask," which is about moving. Some of the moving is physical, some psychological and spiritual, and it is embodied in more than 70 stunning masks, no two alike, from across sub-Saharan Africa. Some were used for popular entertainment; others were moral forces incarnate. Their purpose was transport, and they are supplemented by films of masquerades in action. Hours: Mondays, Thursdays and Fridays, 10 a.m. to 5 p.m.; Saturdays and Sundays, 11 to 6 p.m. Admission: $5; $2.50 for students and 65+ (Holland Cotter).

* "THE FUGITIVES," Riviera, 103 Metropolitan Avenue, at Wythe Avenue, Williamsburg, Brooklyn, www.seeyouattheriviera.com (through Sunday). This new gallery is the collective project of five artists and designers who have plans to show work as cross-disciplinary as their own. They have started on the right foot with a fine group show of seven artists whose paintings and sculptures incorporate images from comics and advertising, the street and the Web. Travis Millard, Lee Misenheimer, Paul Clark, Niko Stumpo, Scien & Klor, Matt Owens and Rick Froeberg are the names in the international lineup, and there isn't a weak link among them (Cotter).

"THE GIFT: GENEROUS OFFERINGS, THREATENING HOSPITALITY," Bronx Museum of the Arts, 1040 Grand Concourse, at 165th Street, Morrisania, (718) 681-6000 (through Sunday). This traveling show has loaded political and social themes about giving, receiving and taking, and a fair amount of interesting art by contemporary figures familiar in New York as well as a passel of youngish Europeans little seen here. It also offers some real gifts -- candy and cotton towels free for the taking; origami sculptures available by barter -- as well as some inhospitable entries, including a doormat that spells out "Welcome" with thousands of upright needles. Hours: Wednesdays, noon to 9 p.m.; Thursdays through Sundays, noon to 6 p.m. Admission: $5; $3, students and 65+; free on Wednesdays (Cotter).

"ADOLPH GOTTLIEB: A SURVEY EXHIBITION," Jewish Museum, 1109 Fifth Avenue, at 92nd Street, (212) 423-3271 (through Sunday). Gottlieb's painting has not aged as well as that of his colleagues Pollock, Rothko and Newman. His journey leads from 1930's representational pictures that resemble those by his friend Milton Avery to the gridded pictographic works of the 40's to his signature circle-and-burst paintings of the late 50's, which have been his most memorable works. In the 1960's his canvases expand and brighten under the influence of Color Field painting. This 31-painting exhibition casts an illuminating light on a period of tremendous creative ferment in Western art. Hours: Sundays, 10 a.m. to 5:45 p.m.; Mondays through Wednesdays, 11 a.m. to 5:45 p.m.; Thursdays, 11 a.m. to 8 p.m.; Fridays, 11 a.m. to 3 p.m. Admission: $8; $5.50 for students and 65+ (Johnson).

* CHRISTIAN HOLSTAD, "Life Is a Gift," Daniel Reich, 308 West 21st Street, Chelsea, (917) 408-9097 (through today). Functioning like a one-man collective, this talented artist makes his debut with a beautiful plastic-enclosed bedroom inspired by the short life and physical isolation of David Vetter, the bubble boy. It is also an extended commentary on craft and recycling, which includes knitted sculpture, quilting, a linoleum rug, jewellike color-pencil drawings, sweetly pornographic collages and, perhaps best of all, "erased" drawings made from newspaper photographs that create a haunted world inhabited by vulnerable creatures and spirits (Smith).

* "MODERN AND CONTEMPORARY PORTRAITS," Forum Gallery, 745 Fifth Avenue, at 57th Street, (212) 355-4545 (through tomorrow). A formidable assemblage of faces has been assembled for this ambitious portrait jam, organized by Townsend Wolfe, former director and chief curator of the Arkansas Art Center in Little Rock. The subjects range from a demure but vivid young thing known as "Mady" (1948) by Alice Neel to the belligerent countenance of the painter Francesco Clemente, seen in a woodcut done by him in 1982. In between are close to 60 others, some quirky and antic and others ultra-traditional (Glueck).

"THE POINTED PEN: WILLIAM SHARP'S COURTROOM DRAWINGS, POLITICAL CARICATURES AND BOOK ILLUSTRATIONS FROM THE 1930'S-1950'S," Queens Museum of Art, New York City Building, Flushing Meadows-Corona Park (718) 592-9700 (through Sunday). A wizard with a pen, Sharp (1900-1961), an Austrian-born Jew who satirized Hitler and his followers as they rose to power in the 1920's, fled Germany in 1933 and spent the rest of his life in Forest Hills, Queens. From there, this caricaturist, political cartoonist, courtroom artist, book illustrator and vignettist of urban life took the world as his subject matter, zeroing in on subjects ranging from World War II and the cold war to bums, boxers and baseball players. The more than 250 drawings, etchings and book illustrations here attest to the amazing breadth and engagement of Sharp's work. Part of its charm is the relative innocence of its time and the innocence of Sharp's vision, too, more a journalistic than an artistic one, but nonetheless powerful. Hours: Tuesdays through Fridays, 10 a.m. to 5 p.m.; Saturdays and Sundays, noon to 5 p.m. Admission: $5; $ 2, students and 55+ (Glueck).

HEADLINE: ON STAGE AND OFF 

BYLINE:  By Jesse McKinley 

 BODY:
Reason for Hope In Union Talks
 Producers and the musicians' union negotiated again yesterday after an exchange of unpleasantries in dueling news conferences on Tuesday. How much progress was made was uncertain, but though a deal may not be in place by the deadline on Sunday, a full strike was considered unlikely. 
 The main reason for optimism seemed to be a prevailing notion that neither side would benefit from a Broadway shutdown, and that producers are especially vulnerable with a fragile economy and a prospect of war. 
"Nobody can afford it," said one major producer, speaking on condition of anonymity. "The musicians should write a thank you note to George Bush."
The League of American Theaters and Producers, which represents the producers, said that minimums, the set number of musicians at every Broadway musical, remained the fundamental issue. "Everything else revolves around this," said Jed Bernstein, the president of the league. He added that no compromise -- say, a reduction of minimums, not full elimination -- had been offered.
The talks are continuing, but the casts of musicals have started rehearsing with "virtual orchestras," computer programs that simulate the sound of a live orchestra. It isn't always smooth sailing.
Jeffrey Seller, a producer of "La Boheme," confirmed that rehearsals had been slowed by computer tics. "Trying to set tempos with a computer program is a difficult process," Mr. Seller said. "Anytime there's going to be new programs, there's going to be bugs."
Still, like many producers, Mr. Seller tried to be positive. He canceled rehearsals with the computer today in the hope that an agreement was near. "It's an expensive rehearsal," he said, "and I prefer to remain optimistic that they're going to get a deal over the weekend."
Even producers who thought a strike possible expected it to be short. "I don't think they will strike on March 3," one said. "But I think there will be a work action, probably a very short walkout."
For their part, leaders of Local 802 of the American Federation of Musicians planned on pamphleting theaters this weekend. Shawn Sachs, a spokesman for the union, said reports of computer snafus only hardened their resolve. "Nothing concrete has changed," said Mr. Sachs. "We continue our call to protect live music on Broadway and we believe the public is behind us."

First Biltmore Season
The Manhattan Theater Club has settled on the three shows that will play at its new Broadway home, the renovated Biltmore Theater (price tag: $35 million) on West 47th Street.
Surprisingly, the first Biltmore season will not include a play by Terrance McNally, who has been closely associated with the group for many years. Mr. McNally's new play, the first act of which had a recent reading, is not ready for production, a theater spokesman said. Instead the Biltmore's premiere production will be by another regular: Richard Greenberg, the prolific playwright whose drama "Take Me Out" opened on Broadway last night. (Review, Page 3.) Mr. Greenberg's newest one is "The Violet Hour," a drama about a young publisher and a mysterious machine, which will open at the Biltmore in October.
That will be followed by "Drowning Crow," Regina Taylor's adaptation of "The Sea Gull" by Chekhov, which, like its source material, follows the travails of a group of artists on retreat. The Biltmore's inaugural year will end with a revival of "Sight Unseen," Donald Margulies's dark comedy about a fashionable painter and his unsettled past. The play, a hit for Manhattan Theater Club in 1992, will arrive on Broadway next spring.

Three Shows Moving
You have to hand it to them: Off Broadway producers are nothing if not optimistic. Despite a rough season so far, three current shows announced transfers this week to longer runs. The biggest upgrade is "Hank Williams: Lost Highway," which will transfer from the Manhattan Ensemble Theater in SoHo to the new Little Shubert Theater on West 42nd Street. The show, a sell-out at the 140-seat theater downtown, will be looking to fill all of the Li'l Shub's 499 seats. Performances begin March 25.
"The Blacks," which has been drawing crowds to the Classical Theater of Harlem for a month, will head downtown to the home of Classic Stage Company, on East 13th Street, on March 11 for a four-week run. It is the four-year-old nonprofit troupe's first transfer. Also moving is Jonathan Tolins's "Last Sunday in June," a Rattlestick Theater production that will transfer to the Century Center for the Performing Arts off Union Square after its run at 224 Waverly Place in Greenwich Village ends in March.

Humor in Letters
For those who came of age after the McCarthy era and don't have a degree in film, the name Dalton Trumbo may mean little. So a quick primer: he was a big-time Hollywood screenwriter whose career was interrupted when he was subpoenaed in 1947 -- as one of the Hollywood 10 -- to appear before the House Committee on Un-American Activities to testify about his political beliefs. Refusing to name names, Trumbo spent a year in prison in 1950 and the next decade in blacklisted exile before returning to write such seminal movies as "Spartacus" and "Papillon." He died in 1976.
Trumbo was also, as it turns out, an avid and witty letter writer. Beginning March 31, those letters will form the backbone of a new theater piece, "Trumbo," which has attracted four big-name Hollywood stars -- Ed Harris, Tim Robbins, Richard Dreyfuss and Chris Cooper -- to do one-night stands in the show. The piece, assembled by Trumbo's son Christopher, will play Mondays only at the Westside Theater, 407 West 43rd Street. Proceeds will benefit People for the American Way, a civil liberties group.
Mr. Harris, who is flying in for the day from his home in Los Angeles, said the decision was simple. "It's about something important: it's about free speech," he said. "And it's actually pretty funny, too."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dalton Trumbo and his wife, Cleo, in 1947. His correspondence is the basis for a new theater piece to open on West 43rd Street. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: JOURNEYS; 
Family Resorts: Part Idyll, Part Survival Test

BYLINE:  By NANCY M. BETTER 

 BODY:
AT 5 p.m. the day after Christmas, my family arrived at the Grand Wailea Hotel on Maui, bleary-eyed from flying to Hawaii, halfway around the world from our home in Connecticut. Outside, white-uniformed porters scurried around with overloaded trolleys of luggage; inside, the gleaming marble lobby was teeming with people. While waiting to check in, we noticed a curious phenomenon: everyone, staff members and guests alike, seemed to be carrying walkie-talkies.
 It did not take long to figure out why. The Grand Wailea has 40 acres, 780 rooms, 6 restaurants and 5 bars, and it is easy to get lost on its lushly manicured grounds. Bewildered newcomers can be spotted checking their maps. It took us three days to find the quickest route to the beach; it involved two elevators, one flight of stairs and a quarter-mile hike. 
 I wish we had known to bring along walkie-talkies, but then, I wish we had known lots of things about our dream vacation before booking it 15 months earlier. (The hotel recommends making peak-season reservations at least a year in advance.) 
This was our first visit to an action-packed mega-resort, aside from Walt Disney World. Our friends raved about these places, and our children begged to go. Their classmates would come home from the holidays sporting cool plastic wristbands, each a different color signifying the length of their stay at places like the Grand Wailea or the Atlantis Paradise Island in the Bahamas. They would boast of towering water slides and thundering whitewater rapids.
We had also been hearing about a major renovation at the venerable Breakers Hotel in Palm Beach, Fla., that made it a magnet for families. Paul Leone, the president of the Breakers, said the resort's management decided a decade ago to make the property less formal, to capitalize on the fast-growing market for upscale family travel. 
So when we realized we had enough frequent flyer miles to justify taking a family of five (two adults and three children, aged 10, 8 and 6) 5,000 miles, we decided to take the plunge. We chose the Grand Wailea not for its three championship golf courses or its award-winning spa but for its spectacular waterworks. Since 1991, the hotel has lavished more than $25 million on a high-tech network of nine pools, including a one-of-a-kind water elevator. We envisioned ourselves lounging on the beach while the children frolicked in this aquatic theme park.
But we soon discovered that a successful trip to a mega-resort like the Grand Wailea requires survival of the fittest. During peak season (roughly mid-November to April), nearly every pool chaise is draped in towels by the crack of dawn, all aerobic machines in the gym are occupied by 8 a.m., every restaurant is heavily booked months in advance, and the steady stream of surcharges threatens to send an already expensive vacation into the financial stratosphere.
In the end, we managed to make the most of our trip, and the children had a ball. And if we ever go back to one of these mega-resorts, we will go armed with knowledge earned the hard way. Consider these the Rules of the Jungle, 10 things every parent should know before booking such a trip.

1. Children Stay Free?
It all depends on how you define "free." Yes, children 17 and under can stay at no charge in their parents' rooms at the Grand Wailea. But other incidental costs can mount up fast. Book a pair of adjoining rooms, and you are charged a connecting fee of $30 a night. If two of your children refuse to sleep together, you will need to add a cot for $25 a night. Throw in a refrigerator rental -- so you can leave the $5 sodas locked up in the mini-bar -- for $25, plus taxes on all these extras, and you will find an extra $400 tacked onto your final bill. (The Grand Wailea has a six-night minimum stay during peak season.)
At the Atlantis, the charge for children 12 and older who stay in their parents' rooms is $45 a night per child. To encourage them to stay next door, the resort can guarantee adjoining rooms and does not charge a connecting fee.
The Breakers allows children 16 and under to stay in their parents' rooms at no charge; cots are free but must be requested before arrival.

2. Location, Location, Location
The huge Atlantis Paradise Island resort has 11 swimming areas, a 50,000-square-foot casino, a full-service spa, an 18-hole golf course, 10 tennis courts, a shopping mall and a conference center. Waterways filled with sharks, tropical fish and stingrays course throughout the 34-acre property.
Upon booking, guests are sent a CD-ROM with an interactive resort map; practice in tracing routes presumably helps prevent disorientation upon arrival. Atlantis veterans offer one piece of advice: Choose your accommodations carefully. With 2,317 rooms, some guests are housed so far from the main pool area that they need to travel by shuttle bus.
"You have to request the Royal Towers, because it is closest to the action," said Victoria Stafford, who lives in Fairfield County, Conn., and has four young children. "You also have to be prepared to chase your kids around, because it's hard to keep track of them."
At the Grand Wailea, we stayed in the Molokini Wing, which is farthest from the pools and beach but closest to the shopping mall and golf courses. We were assigned this location because it was available; this turned out to be a good thing because this is the quiet side of the hotel, a boon during the all-night New Year's Eve beach party.

3. Don't Be Captive to Crowds
If the hectic scene at the Atlantis threatens to ruin your sanity, there is another option: stay at the nearby Ocean Club, a more tranquil, secluded and intimate hotel (with only 106 rooms) owned by the same company.
Marlene Baum, a New York mother of two, recommends this strategy. "The Ocean Club is lovely and civilized, with wonderful service," she said. "We stay there, and then take our kids to Atlantis for lunch and a couple of hours in the pools."
Shuttles between the two resorts operate continuously, or you can take a 15-minute stroll down the shared beach.

4. Early Birds Get the Chaise
The Breakers, a palatial Italian villa built in 1896, is far from the carnival atmosphere of the island resorts, although its offerings are comprehensive: 560 rooms, 4 pools, 2 18-hole golf courses and a 20,000-square-foot spa.
The environment at the Breakers may be elegant -- croquet and horseshoes are popular activities -- but beneath the genteel Gatsbyesque veneer, guests have to hustle for everything.
One New York executive recruiter who asked that her name not be used to protect her privacy raced down to the pool at 6:45 each morning to claim chaises in a certain spot. "I like the first-come, first-served system," she said. "One year we went to the Four Seasons on Nevis, and they assigned us chairs for the whole week. We arrived on Dec. 27th and got horrible chairs. Everyone else had been there since before Christmas. It wasn't until after New Year's that we finally got decent chairs."

5. High-Priced Child's Play
Children's programs can make golf lessons and spa treatments look cheap.
At the Breakers' Coconut Crew Interactive Camp, children aged 3 to 12 pay $70 for a six-hour day. In addition to tennis, golf, soccer and swimming, the camp features the Secret Garden, a 1,200-foot-long hedge maze, and a beachfront playground.
For $75 a day at the Grand Wailea's Camp Grande, children can be entertained with swimming, scavenger hunts, and arts and crafts, along with Hawaiian specialties like hula dancing, ukulele lessons and lei making. Teenage guests can work as C.I.T.'s (counselors in training), paying $15 and $25 for the privilege of assisting Camp Grande's staff.
Of course, many children (mine included) will forgo camp in favor of hanging out at a big resort's bustling waterfront. At the Grand Wailea, boogie boards and kayaks can be rented by the hour ($5 for the boards, $15 and $25 for the kayaks, depending on the size). Our children begged for surfing lessons ($59 for 90 minutes) and windsurfing lessons ($69 for 90 minutes). Even bouncing on the water trampoline anchored in the ocean cost $10 per child.

6. Plan Ahead or Lose Out
Upon our arrival at the Grand Wailea, we asked the concierge about a reservation at the resort's Japanese restaurant. "Tonight?" she responded, incredulous. We then inquired about signing up for massages the next afternoon. She explained, with more than a hint of impatience in her voice, that there were no openings available for the next two weeks.
"Your trip will go a lot more smoothly if you plan in advance," said Neesha Lahiri, marketing coordinator for the Grand Wailea. She recommends making reservations for meals, as well as spa treatments, day camp and child care, several weeks in advance of a visit.
(If you find yourself shut out, refuse to take no for an answer, at least from the resort's concierge. Eager to visit the Old Lahaina Luau, a Maui attraction, we asked the concierge for assistance. She immediately informed us that it was fully booked throughout our stay. We called the luau directly and found it had had a cancellation for the following evening.)

7. Repeaters Know the Ropes
On our first day at the Grand Wailea we ran into some hometown friends who make an annual pilgrimage to the hotel. They proved invaluable in helping us navigate around the resort. They suggested that we skip the extravagant buffet breakfast ($23.50 for adults, $11.75 for children 5 to 12) in favor of a coffee shop in the mall next door. And they recommended that we stop using the resort's slow-moving valet service and opt for self-parking in a nearby garage.
We still had to pay the valet fee (it is a mandatory charge added to each room bill), but at least we avoided a long wait for our car each day.

8. Meal-Plan Prisoners
The Atlantis suggests that guests 12 and older buy dining plans. Children 4 and under are free, but only when their parents dine at the same time. That means adding lots of $15 grilled-cheese sandwiches to your room-service bill if you leave the children behind with a baby sitter.
The resort's Modified American Plan ($55 per adult per day, $30 per child per day) offers breakfast in four restaurants and dinner in six restaurants. Want to upgrade to the Gourmet Dining Plan ($79 per adult per day, $41 per child per day) with breakfast in five restaurants and dinner in 13 restaurants? You are out of luck; this plan cannot be bought at the resort.
"We require advance booking for this plan because it helps us schedule accordingly at our more expensive restaurants," said Ashley McBain, a spokeswoman for the resort. "We recommend that guests choose a meal plan, because it ends up being more economical, for both adults and children." A 15 percent mandatory tip is automatically added at each meal.

9. Takeout Options
One second-generation Breakers guest from Westchester County says she and her family like the resort but not the food. So she orders sandwiches and salads from a local deli and picks them up at the front desk. She also requests doggy bags when dining at Palm Beach's top restaurants and asks the kitchen staff to heat up leftovers for her children's dinner.
Mr. Leone, the Breakers president, acknowledges that "lunchtime can be challenging for families during the busy season." Over this past Presidents' Day weekend, his staff set up an extra 100-seat cafe on the beach to ease crowding and provide quick service. "It was costly, but we have to provide the necessary resources for peak periods," he said. He added that the Breakers recently opened a casual, family-style Italian restaurant adjacent to its family entertainment center.

10. Well-Padded Totals
On top of taxes and mandatory tips, the Grand Wailea charges a resort service fee of $15 a day per room. This covers the valet parking, a lei for each arriving guest and other odds and ends. At the Atlantis, adults (including children 12 and over) pay a $7 daily surcharge for utilities and housekeeping tips.

The Bottom Line
"You and your family can have a blast, but you have to realize that you are going to pay five-star resort prices for a place that's a lot like Disney World," said Ms. Stafford, the Fairfield mother of four. "If you have that mind-set, you won't be disappointed."

IF YOU GO

Bring a Swimsuit and Lots of Cash

THE ATLANTIS

Paradise Island, the Bahamas; (954) 809-2100; www.atlantis.com.

RATES -- From March to May 2003, rooms from $340 to $665; suites from $760 to $5,225 a night. There is also a Bridge Suite for $25,000 a night.

CHILDREN'S PROGRAMS -- The Discovery Channel Camp charges $70 a day for ages 4 to 12 and puts an emphasis on education. There is a technology lab with computers and video camcorders, plus a science center with microscopes, touch tanks and specimen collections. Club Rush ($10 for children 8 to 12, 4:30 to 6:30 p.m.; $15 for teenagers, 8 p.m. to 12:30 a.m.) is a clubhouse with Internet access, video games, big-screen television movies and music. There is also an 18-hole putting course.

THE BREAKERS

1 South County Road, Palm Beach, Fla.; (561) 655-6611; www.thebreakers.com.

RATES -- From March to May 2003, rooms from $435 to $835; suites from $745 to $3,400 a night.

CHILDREN'S PROGRAMS -- The Breakers offers the Coconut Crew Interactive Camp, which serves ages 3 to 12, for $70 a day. Activities include golf and tennis, as well as seashell hunting and kite flying. Older children can try scuba diving, kayaking expeditions and snorkeling.

GRAND WAILEA HOTEL

3850 Wailea Alanui Drive, Wailea, Hawaii; (808) 875-1234; www.grandwailea.com.

RATES -- From March to May 2003, rooms from $450 to $800; suites from $1,575 to $10,000 a night.

CHILDREN'S PROGRAMS -- Camp Grande, which offers programs for ages 5 to 12, charges $75 a day. Activities include table tennis, swimming, and arts and crafts, along with Hawaiian specialties like hula dancing, ukulele lessons and lei making. There are also a children's computer center and a wading pool.

SECTION: Section E; Part 1; Column 4; Movies, Performing Arts/Weekend Desk; Pg. 1

LENGTH: 2873 words

HEADLINE: Ending Its Mega-Run, 'Les Miz' Comes Full Circle 

BYLINE:  By ROBIN POGREBIN 

 BODY:
TERRENCE MANN was the first Inspector Javert in the Broadway production of "Les Miserables." Now, 16 years later, he has come back to be the last.
 On May 18 this blockbuster version of the Victor Hugo novel that helped define the mega-musical of the 1980's will go dark at the Imperial Theater, taking its place in the record books as the second longest-running Broadway show of all time, after "Cats." 
 While people on Broadway who do eight shows a week usually look at each night as just one taxing performance more, these days the cast and crew of "Les Miserables" look at each night as one treasured performance less.
One show less for Carmel Vargyas, the hair and makeup supervisor, to comb and tease the graying ponytail wig that the villainous Javert wears as he pursues the big-hearted fugitive Jean Valjean. One show less for the men in the cast to gather in a circle onstage for a bonding game of hacky sack -- keeping a beanbag in the air with various body parts -- just before curtain time. One show less for Ron Fedeli, the head carpenter, to test the turntable on which the bloodied barricades revolve in battle. One show less for the actors to lie onstage together in the dark, pretending to sleep as they listen to the wrenching ballad "Bring Him Home." 
With the closing of "Cats" in September 2000 and of "Miss Saigon" in January 2001, the closing of "Les Miz" inevitably seems heavy with symbolism, like the end of an era. This leaves "The Phantom of the Opera" as the last remaining big British import, which will soon become the third longest-running show in Broadway history.
"It's a loss to the skyline of Broadway," said Nick Wyman, who has played Thenardier, the jovial, self-interested innkeeper in "Les Miz," since the show's 10th anniversary in 1997.
Mr. Mann said the end of "Les Miz" signaled the beginning of a new chapter in musical theater.
"I don't think we'll see mega-musicals anymore," he said. "I don't think we'll see things run 12 years, 9 years, 5 years. Even if you take 9/11 out of the equation, the economics make it difficult.
"As in all areas of theater and art, there are cycles, and now we're in a cycle when we have to figure out what the next genre is. It always happens after wars or tragedy."
Directed by Trevor Nunn and John Caird, "Les Miz" won eight Tony Awards in 1987, including best musical. By the time it closes, the Broadway show will have been seen by more than nine million people and grossed more than $400 million at the box office, employed 421 actors and 1,633 support staff members and fathered 53 productions in 38 countries in 21 languages.
The production in London, where the musical started in 1985, is still running.
Cameron Mackintosh, the show's producer, said of it: "I thought it would last two or three years. I thought it might be another serious musical like 'Evita.' I had no idea it would sail past 'The Sound of Music.' "
Mr. Mann came back to the show at the request of Mr. Mackintosh, who also created "Phantom," "Miss Saigon" and "Cats," in which Mr. Mann originated the role of Rum Tum Tugger on Broadway.
Mr. Mann said "Les Miz" was the more momentous musical. "This epic story of man's indomitability and redemption," he said, "was even bigger than 'Cats.' "
Being present at the arrival of "Les Miz," experiencing its impact firsthand, was both uplifting and burdensome. "It was overwhelming," Mr. Mann said. "My reaction to it all was: 'Oh, my gosh, there's so much to live up to.' The pressure of this being the seminal piece of musical theater for the century.
"There was 'Showboat' in the 20's, which turned a corner. Then there was 'Oklahoma!' and then this. The pressure made it difficult to enjoy sometimes."
Mr. Mann said he returned to close out "Les Miz" more for selfish than sentimental reasons. He was in the show for eight months the first time around, leaving to do two movies, "Critters 2: The Main Course" and "Big Top Pee-wee."
He wanted another shot at the role, a chance to do it better with the benefit of age and experience. "When I was 36 and doing the show, I was just running on youthful adrenaline," he said. "Now I'm 51 and trying to find a way to enjoy doing eight shows a week."
(Mr. Mann never went back to "Cats." "You can only fit into that spandex once in your life," he said.)
So far, Mr. Mann said, he could describe the experience only as strange. "It feels incredibly familiar and incredibly unfamiliar, all at the same time," he said.
"It's hard not to just drop back into the slot of the way I did it -- perceived it -- before," he continued. "That's the hardest thing, to make it new."
To prepare, he said, he listened to the original Broadway recording (featuring him), saw the show about four times and went into rehearsal. "By the end of that first rehearsal, I knew 85 percent of the show again," he said. "It kind of just came back into me."

A Rite of Passage
There are those who never left and those who have come and gone. Mr. Wyman, the innkeeper, described "Les Miz" as a rite of passage for actors. "When I came into the city, it was 'Godspell' or 'Grease,' and everyone passed through one of those two shows," he said. "Now everyone comes through 'Les Miz,' either Broadway or the road."
Over his decade in the show, Mr. Wyman has seen many cast members come and go. "I've had three different wives," he said, "each one wonderful."
Like many associated with "Les Miz," Mr. Wyman said it was the substance of the musical that held his interest after all these years, with its themes of love and destruction and second chances. The show, which depicts the French student uprisings of 1832 and the years leading up to them, features music by Claude-Michel Schonberg and lyrics by Herbert Kretzmer, with original French text by Alain Boublil and Jean-Marc Natel and additional material by James Fenton.
"It's one of the most amazing messages of redemption," Mr. Wyman said. "It also hammers away at the idea of parenthood -- fathers and daughters, mothers and daughters, fathers and sons."
Mr. Wyman pointed to the song "Bring Him Home," in which Valjean sings affectingly about Marius, who isn't even his son. "For anyone who has children, it's very moving," Mr. Wyman said.
"Les Miz" has helped Mr. Wyman support his three children in a career that has also included teaching, television and films like "Die Hard With a Vengeance."
"You cobble together your living from all sorts of sources," Mr. Wyman said. "It has been wonderful to have a steady commitment."
Because of the show's weighty themes, Mr. Wyman said, he was also grateful to be the one who supplies some of the show's few lighter moments, like the song, "Master of the House."
"I am blessed with having the best role," he said. "In the midst of torment, there is one bright ray of comic sunshine and that's me."
Mr. Wyman is one of the men who play hacky sack before the show. Legend has it that this "Les Miz" preshow tradition was established by Glenn Calhoun, a former prop man. The actors said the game creates a sense of camaraderie and could be a metaphor for the democratic nature of the production.
"I've done 'Phantom' and 'Les Miz,' " Mr. Wyman said. "In 'Phantom,' you've got singers and dancers and featured roles and stars. Here we are together. We are field hands and convicts. We all start off together as members of the ensemble."

Keeping Interest Up
This ensemble quality started with the show's beginning at the Royal Shakespeare Company's Barbican Theater in London in October 1985. "Les Miz" transferred to the Palace Theater in the West End in December 1985. It then tried out at the Kennedy Center in Washington in 1986 and moved to Broadway in 1987, starting at the Broadway Theater and moving to the Imperial in 1990.
Ms. Vargyas, the hair and makeup supervisor, has worked on long-running shows before, but nothing compares to "Les Miz," where she has worked since 1988. She did the 1987 revival of "Cabaret" for a couple of years but quickly grew tired of it. "After six weeks I said, 'I can't listen to this music one more time,' " she said. "But here, I'm not there yet.
"There's an emotional attachment to the show that has been like no other, so I have not thought about leaving. I am now."
The crew members seem to be looking for work only halfheartedly. They are in no rush. They know something this solid and this satisfying is unlikely to come along again.
"I've loved every minute," said Gregg N. Kirsopp, the production stage manager, who has been with the show more than 11 years. "I've had opportunities to go do some other things. This is just a great, powerful story, and I love working with these people."
"It's just a wonderful message that we're putting out there," he continued. "That there is good in all."
Mr. Kirsopp can chart the development of his family by the run of "Les Miz." His wife was a production stage manager on the show before him, though they were married before "Les Miz" began. Their son, 7, saw the show two years ago. "He wants to see it again before it closes," Mr. Kirsopp said. "He'll probably understand a lot more of it now."
Similarly, Mr. Fedeli, the head carpenter, has seen his children grow up with "Les Miz." When he started on the show in 1987, his oldest son was 18 months old. He will be 17 by the time the show closes.
"This has been a tremendous job opportunity," Mr. Fedeli said. "I also look at it as a time to move on to whatever is next."
He doesn't know what's next but assumes it will be another Broadway show. The business runs in his blood. Mr. Fedeli's grandfather and father were stagehands. His brothers are, too. He met his wife, a former stage manager, on "Evita." They were married in 1989.
Mr. Fedeli started on "Les Miz" as the automation man, or turntable operator, and worked his way up to head carpenter. Running the turntable may not seem like much, but in "Les Miz" it is essential, moving actors and scenery on and off stage, intensifying the drama of the show by its rapid revolutions.

The Compliment of Parody
It has also been a ripe target for parody, as evidenced by the long-running spoof "Forbidden Broadway," which has a "Les Miz" number featuring actors constantly rotating as they sing to the tune of "At the End of the Day":
At the end of the play
We're another year older
And we're often exhausted from playing the poor
Enjolras fell in the band
And the turntable's making us dizzy
Trevor Nunn yells a command
And he's throwing us all in a tizzy
And there's gonna be hell to pay
At the end of the play!
Despite the ridicule, Mr. Mann said he never felt the need to apologize for the show. "I think anything that becomes caricatured, satirized certainly has to have some validity," he said. "You can't make fun of something unless people have taken it seriously. That is the essence of satire."
Gerard Alessandrini, the creator of "Forbidden Broadway," said he would miss "Les Miz." "It's probably one of the most spoofable shows because it's so grand and has all those miserable characters," he said.
Toward the end of the show's run, Mr. Alessandrini said, he plans to put together a special version of "Forbidden Broadway" consisting of all his "Les Miz" numbers. He has created a new one each year. Already "Forbidden Broadway" has been performing a countdown to the closing of "Les Miz" that takes off on the show's song "One Day More." It is called "Three Months More" and will eventually become "Two Months More" and so on. "The day before the show closes," Mr. Alessandrini said, "it won't be a spoof anymore."
A sample:
Soon we'll close and please each rival
But "Les Miz" fans quell your fears
We'll be back as a revival
That will run for 40 years.
For Randal Keith, the end of "Les Miz" is a beginning. For years he has been hoping to play Valjean on Broadway. Since 1997 he has performed the part in Shanghai, Korea, Toronto and across the United States. Now he is finally getting his shot at it. Mr. Mackintosh brought him in to replace J. Mark McVey as Valjean after the original star, Colm Wilkinson, could not do it because of a family illness. But Mr. Keith's Broadway debut will last all of 12 weeks. Still, Mr. Keith said he was grateful for it. Better a brief Broadway run than no Broadway run at all. "It's something I've been waiting for," he said.
"I think it's one of the most vocally demanding roles I've ever had," he added. "Three hours of solid singing takes a lot out of you."

Filling the Downtime
At three hours, the show is epic in scope, and many members of the cast and crew have patches of downtime. In the wings the actors stretch, whisper or sit quietly while they wait to go on. In the basement they play cards, touch up their makeup and chat in their white terry cloth "Les Miserables" robes. Their lockers are decorated with family photos and clippings. They brush their teeth side by side at the sink. It feels like a fraternity.
The actors have their private jokes, even onstage. At one point in the show, David McDonald, who has been with "Les Miz" on and off since 1995, and two of his fellow actors sing three-part harmony under their breath behind the barricade. It's a song they sing for themselves. "It's beautiful, actually," Mr. McDonald said. "Too bad the audience can't hear it."
In the wardrobe department where Adelaide Laurino presides among the washing machines, there is a stack of videotapes piled next to a small television, with titles like "Working Girl," "Small Time Crooks" and "Get Shorty."
"It's a chicken coop is what it is," Ms. Laurino said of the cramped underground space where she is surrounded by clothes to be laundered, pressed or mended.
The stage manager's office near the stage door has become a gathering place. Regular contests are conducted there: best hair, most changed, most likely to . . . Cast members bring in photographs of themselves and cast their ballots. The entries are posted on the wall.

The Children Play Games
The children in the show are considerably more creative with their time offstage. There are five in the show; the girls alternate performances of young Cosette and young Eponine, the boys play Gavroche. Many of them are offstage from intermission until the curtain call. Jim Cleveland, chaperone for the last 14 years, helps them find activities, assists with homework, arbitrates disputes.
But the children are rarely at loose ends. Over the years they have developed a rich fantasy world.Their make-believe games include apartment, streets, puppies, orphanage, business, jail and college. They keep a box full of dress-up clothes. "We pretend we are in a battle," said Christiana Anbri, 12, who has been in the show four years. "I look forward to coming here. I say to myself, 'One more hour till I go to the theater.' "
Alexandra Rose Sullivan, 9, who joined the show last year, said, "These are my best friends here."
The longer they stay, the children say, the more attached they become to the production and to one another. In addition to Christiana and Alexandra, the group currently includes Andrew Hoeft, Nicholas Jonas and Kristin Danielle Klabunde.
"I've seen 14 Gavroches and 15 Cosettes leave," Christiana said. "There were a few kids I got really close to."
When Mr. Cleveland talked about the children who have come and gone, tears rolled down his cheeks. He says it is hard to imagine doing something else after so many years of doing this. "I was a teacher before," he said. "I stayed because of the kids."
Over the years, most of the 87 children who have been in "Les Miz" have left because they outgrew their roles. Now the show is leaving them. "I'm really sad, but I'm also happy I'm not leaving because I'm too big or something," Christiana said. "I'm leaving with the whole entire company."

The Last Words
Certainly "Les Miz" has had its rough patches. There was the time in 1996 when Mr. Mackintosh replaced much of the cast after deciding that the production had grown stale.
But on the whole, those who have been associated with "Les Miz" feel that they have helped make Broadway history. "It's such an honor to be a part of it," said Edward Juvier, who plays Joly and has been in the show on and off since 2000. "Many people joined theater because of the show. I did. I saw it in this theater. I remember the exact seat I was sitting in."
Inevitably, members of the cast and crew said, the final days in May will be emotional. "I'm sure everyone's going to be walking around in tears," Ms. Vargyas said. "It's not a shock that it's closing. It's been coming for a while. But it will be an adjustment. A big adjustment."
Everything will take on a new meaning, particularly the lyrics. Like "Drink with me to days gone by/Sing with me the songs we knew" and the show's final words that on closing night will bring "Les Miserables" to an end:
Will you join in our crusade
Who will be strong and stand with me?
Somewhere beyond the barricade
Is there a world you long to see?
Do you hear the people sing
Say, do you hear the distant drums?
It is the future that they bring
When tomorrow comes.

Final Days
"Les Miserables" is at the Imperial Theater, 249 West 45th Street, (212) 239-6200. Performances are Tuesdays at 7 p.m.; Thursdays and Fridays at 8 p.m.; Wednesdays and Saturdays at 2 and 8 p.m.; Sundays at 3 p.m. Tickets: $20 to $85. This musical is scheduled to close on May 18.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS; 
Shares Rally After Government Reduces Terrorism Alert

BYLINE:  By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Relieved that the government lowered the national terror alert level and heartened by a report that Iraq would destroy some missiles, investors pushed shares higher yesterday, reversing much of the previous session's big sell-off.
 Positive economic news -- a report of an unexpectedly strong increase in orders to factories for big-ticket items -- also fed the advance. 
 Still, analysts remained dubious about chances for a sustained upward trend in the face of war fears. 
"Until this Iraq situation is resolved, the market is always going to be running the risk of getting into this war without knowing what it means" to the economy, Barry Berman, head trader for Robert W. Baird & Company in Milwaukee, said. 
The Dow Jones industrial average closed up 78.01 points, or 1 percent, at 7,884.99, recovering much of Wednesday's loss of 102.52 points. 
The broader market also rallied. The Nasdaq composite index rose 20.28 points, or 1.6 percent, to 1,323.96. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 9.73 points, or 1.2 percent, to 837.28.
Wall Street seemed encouraged by reports that the Bush administration was lowering the terror alert level to yellow from orange, the second-highest status, suggesting that the threat of a terrorist attack had eased. 
The market also took heart from a report by Egypt's news agency that Iraq would announce later in the day that it would comply with a United Nations order to begin destroying its Samoud 2 missiles by the weekend. But no such comment came from the Iraqi government.
Also yesterday, the Commerce Department reported that orders to factories for durable goods rose 3.3 percent in January, the best showing in six months.
Still, analysts doubted the market's ability to sustain any gains, expecting investors to hold back until it is clearer if and when there will be a war and what effect it will have on the economy. 
"Any up day is a good day these days, but I am not giving it too much weight," said Gary Kaltbaum, market technician for Investors' Edge Partners in Orlando, Fla. "There is still a lot of work to be done in this market. It is still trying to hammer out a low."
Shares of major manufacturers closed higher on the durable goods report. DaimlerChrysler rose 73 cents, to $30.07, while Maytag advanced 61 cents, to $24.11.
Better-than-anticipated earnings and brokerage house upgradings gave other stocks a lift. The PG&E Corporation, the energy company, rose 42 cents, to $13.19, on fourth-quarter profits that beat analysts' expectations by 3 cents a share.
Darden Restaurants, which operates the Red Lobster and Olive Garden chains, rose $1.05, to $17.85, after J. P. Morgan raised its rating to overweight from neutral. 
But Wall Street was disappointed by a report from the Commerce Department that showed that sales of new homes plunged 15.1 percent last month after a 4.1 percent increase in December. Investors sold off home builders, including Centex, which closed down 17 cents, at $55.56. 
In the bond market, the 10-year Treasury note increased 9/32, to a price of 101 5/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 3.74 percent from 3.77 percent. 
The 30-year bond increased 11/32, to 110 5/32. Its yield fell to 4.72 percent from 4.74 percent.

HEADLINE: Economic Data Point in Both Directions 

BYLINE:  AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Feb. 27 

 BODY:
Sales of new homes plunged in January at the fastest pace in nine years, while orders for durable goods posted the biggest rise in six months, reports from the Commerce Department indicated today. 
 The sale of new single-family homes fell to a seasonally adjusted annual pace of 914,000, a 15.1 percent drop from December, when sales reached a record monthly level. 
 The performance, much weaker than analysts had expected, represented the worst single month for new-home sales since January 1994, and, economists said, raised some new questions about consumers' willingness to make big financial commitments amid worries about a war in Iraq. Other economists, minimizing the importance of the decrease, cited the cold weather and seasonal distortions. 
Still, "you can't dismiss sales being off by 15 percent," said David Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders. "I think we are seeing more caution." 
Home sales fell sharply in all parts of the country, except the Northeast, where they rose. Sales in the Midwest dropped 42.2 percent in January, to a pace of 163,000. In the South, sales fell 12.9 percent, to 406,000, and in the West, they dipped 1.4 percent, to a pace of 279,000. But in the Northeast, sales rose 43.5 percent, to a pace of 66,000. 
In a separate report, the Commerce Department said orders to factories for durable goods, relatively costly items expected to last at least three years, rose 3.3 percent in January. The increase came after orders fell 0.4 percent in December and 1.2 percent in November. 
It surpassed the 1 percent advance that economists had predicted and was the strongest performance since July. 
The gains were broad based. Orders for transportation products rose 5.1 percent in January, in contrast to December's 3.5 percent decline. 
Orders for automobiles increased 10.7 percent, the largest increase since April, and a turnaround from a 5.9 percent drop in December. 
Excluding orders for transportation products, which can swing widely from month to month, orders for durable goods rose 2.5 percent in January. 
Orders for computers rose 1.5 percent, down from a 10.4 percent advance in December. For communications equipment, orders soared 46.1 percent, more than erasing a 15.8 percent decline in December. 
Machinery orders rose 5.3 percent, after dropping 1 percent. Orders for primary metals, including steel, grew 4.2 percent, after a 2.6 percent rise.
In still another report, the Labor Department said new claims for unemployment benefits rose by a seasonally adjusted 11,000 last week, to 417,000, a two-month high, suggesting that businesses were inclined to keep work forces lean.
The four-week moving average, which irons out some weekly fluctuations, also rose, to 399,750, from 395,750 the previous week.

HEADLINE: Gap's Results Meet Analysts' Expectations 

BYLINE:  AP 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Feb. 27 

 BODY:
Gap Inc. delivered today the fourth-quarter earnings comeback that management had promised, but warned that its performance this month was not living up to expectations. 
 The company, which is based in San Francisco, earned $248.7 million, or 27 cents a share, in the three months ended Feb. 1 in contrast to a loss of $34.2 million, or 4 cents, in the fourth quarter a year earlier. 
 The results matched the consensus estimate of analysts surveyed by Thomson First Call. Analysts raised the average estimate from 16 cents at the beginning of the month after the company advised that its fourth-quarter profit would range from 23 cents to 29 cents a share. 
Fourth-quarter sales were $4.65 billion, a 14 percent increase from the period a year earlier. Sales at stores open more than a year, a common measure of a retailer's health, rose 8 percent in the quarter. 
The gains reflected a back-to-basics approach Gap adopted in the middle of last year to lure back consumers alienated by selections that emphasized more eclectic fashions. 
The approach helped Gap end 29 consecutive months of sliding sales shortly after a new chief executive, Paul Pressler, replaced its chief, Millard S. Drexler, in September. 
While Gap's February sales at stores open more than a year have been higher than a year ago, the performance was short of management's projections, said Byron Pollitt, the chief financial officer. 
Sagging consumer confidence and poor weather have hurt February sales, Mr. Pollitt said, but "other reasons for this shortfall are difficult to read."

HEADLINE: No Relief in Sight 

BYLINE:  By PAUL KRUGMAN; E-mail: krugman@nytimes.com

 BODY:
So Glenn Hubbard has resigned as chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers -- to spend more time with his family, of course. (Pay no attention to the knife handles protruding from his back.) Gregory Mankiw, his successor, is a very good economist, but never mind: When the political apparatchiks who make all decisions in this administration want Mr. Mankiw's opinion, they'll tell Mr. Mankiw what it is.
 Meanwhile consumer confidence is plunging, and almost two-thirds of voters rate the current state of the economy as "poor." Is there any relief in sight? No. 
 The conventional wisdom among business forecasters now calls for growth of a bit more than 3 percent over the next year. Growth at that pace is barely enough to keep up with rising productivity and an expanding labor force, not enough to make a serious dent in unemployment. And a growing number of forecasters think the conventional wisdom is overoptimistic, that the pain is about to get even worse. 
One reason is the surge in oil prices, which acts like a big tax increase, siphoning off spending that might otherwise have helped create jobs. And forget about those cheerful predictions of cheap oil as soon as Saddam is gone. Yes, oil prices plunged after the 1991 gulf war; but as Philip Verleger of the Council on Foreign Relations points out, back then there were big inventories of oil that got dumped once the crisis was past; this time inventories are very low. And if the victory isn't as quick and easy as promised, or if the aftermath of war is as nasty as many fear, oil prices could easily go much higher.
Then there's the effect of the worst fiscal crisis in the 50 states since World War II. Iris Lav of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities suggests that tax increases and spending cuts at the state level could drain $100 billion from the national economy over the next year. Aid from Washington is an obvious answer -- but the Bush administration refuses to provide a penny.
Finally, the increasingly grim mood of consumers can be a self-fulfilling prophecy. If disheartened families cut their spending, the job picture will worsen even further. 
So where's the upside? To be fair, there are some reasons for hope. Most notably, after two years of low business investment, there's presumably a pent-up demand for new equipment. As I understand it, the prevailing view at the Federal Reserve -- which is considerably more optimistic than private forecasters -- is that once war fears are behind us, business investment will surge, and all will be well with the economy. Sounds like wishful thinking to me, but let's hope they're right. Also, war itself can be an economic stimulus, as the Pentagon replenishes the munitions it uses up in Iraq.
But you don't have to be a doomsayer to feel that the negatives greatly outweigh the positives -- that an economy that is already hurting badly is all too likely to get even worse. So what will the administration do about it? Nothing, of course.
Yes, I know, the Bush team is proposing about $1.5 trillion in tax cuts. But when pressed, administration officials admit that their plan will do little for the economy right now. Why? Because those are long-term cuts; only a tiny fraction of the total will flow into people's pockets this year. Furthermore, most of the tax cuts will -- of course -- go to affluent families, who will probably save most of the money.
Why is the administration so uninterested in helping the economy? Here's my theory: The depressed state of the economy provides a convenient if bogus rationale for the huge, extremely irresponsible long-run tax cuts that, after Iraq, constitute this administration's principal obsession. To do anything else to help the economy would suggest that it's possible to create jobs now without putting the country's future solvency at risk -- and that's not a message this administration wants to convey. 
I almost feel sorry for Mr. Mankiw, who I suspect has no idea what he's getting into; I'm sure he will soon feel frustrated over his inability to have any real influence on this disastrous policy. But on second thought I'll save my sympathy for the two million people who have lost their jobs over the past two years, and are not likely to find new ones any time soon.
